Muslim Knowledge of the Stoic School
Author reviews Muslim historians' mentions of Stoics and the loss of Stoic texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the extent of Muslim knowledge of Stoic philosophy by distinguishing between recognition of the school and access to its texts.

Core Idea
Muslims knew the Stoics by name but lacked their books.

This lesson examines historical references to Stoicism in Islamic sources, highlighting the gap between name recognition and textual transmission. Understanding this gap is crucial for assessing claims about Stoic influence on Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Muslim historians who mention Stoics
Note the names: al-Shahrastani, al-Qifti, the author of al-Asfar al-Arba'a (Mulla Sadra), and the author of Dustur al-'Ulama'. They each provide different descriptions and synonyms for the Stoics.
2
Extract the key terms used
The passage uses multiple terms: al-Riwaqiyyun (Stoics), Ashab al-Mizalla (people of the portico), Ashab al-Ustuwana (people of the pillar). Recognize that these refer to the same school.
3
Note what these sources say about Stoics
Al-Shahrastani names Chrysippus and Zeno. Al-Qifti reports a classification from Hunayn and Farabi. The author of al-Asfar links them to illuminationist philosophy. Dustur al-'Ulama' connects them to Plato's circle.
4
Recognize the absence of translated works
Despite name knowledge, the passage states that no Stoic books appear in lists of translated books. This is compounded by the loss of original Stoic texts in the modern world.
5
Assess implications for Stoic influence
The lack of translated texts raises questions about direct influence. Modern historians disagree on Stoic logic's originality, but without texts, tracing influence is speculative.
This step connects to broader debates in Islamic intellectual history.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن المدرسة الرواقية على العموم فذكر مؤرخو الفكر الفلسفي الإسلامي شيئاً عنها . فالشهرستاني يذكر ( حكماء أهل المظال وهم خروسيس وزينون ) . ويقول القفطي : « إن في تقسيم حنين بن إسحاق وأبي نصر الفارابي لفرق الفلسفة إلى سبع فرق - فرقة هي شيعة كرسيس ، وهم أصحاب المظلة . سموا بذلك لأن تعلمهم كان في رواق هيكل مدينة أثينية » . كما يذكر في فقرة أخرى أنه « كان في زمن جالينوس قوم ينسبون إلى علم ارسطوطاليس ، وهم المسمون بأصحاب المظلة ، وهم الروحانيون » . كما عرفوا أيضاً باسم أصحاب الأسطوانة . ويشير صاحب الأسفار الأربعة إليهم بقوله « إنه أراد أن يجمع أقوال المشائين ونقاوة أهل الإشراق من الحكماء الرواقيين » . ويقسم صاحب دستور العلماء أتباع أفلاطون إلى ثلاثة فرق ، منهم « الرواقيون ، وهم الذين حضروا مجلسه وجلسوا في الرواق واقتبسوا أنوار الحكمة من عباراته واشاراته » . ومع أن المسلمين عرفوا اسم الرواقية - كما رأينا - وأسماء بعض الرواقيين ، إلا أننا لا نجد في قوائم الكتب المترجمة أي ذكر لكتب زينون أو كريزيب أو غيرهما من الرواقيين . ويزيد المسألة صعوبة أن كتب الرواقية نفسها لم تصل إلينا في العالم الحديث - بل فقدت جميعها - ووقف مؤرخو الفلسفة من هذا المنطق مواقف متعارضة . فبينما Zeller و Prantl و Hamelin ينكرون على هذا المنطق كل طرافة - يحاول بروشار أن يثبت طرافة هذا المنطق واستقلاله .
As for the Stoic school in general, historians of Islamic philosophical thought mentioned something about it. Al-Shahrastani mentions 'the philosophers of the tents, namely Chrysippus and Zeno'. Al-Qifti says: 'In the division of the philosophical sects by Hunayn ibn Ishaq and Abu Nasr al-Farabi into seven sects, there is a sect that is the followers of Chrysippus, they are the people of the portico. They were called that because their learning was in the portico of the temple of the city of Athens.' He also mentions in another paragraph that 'In the time of Galen there were people who traced themselves to the science of Aristotle, and they were called the people of the portico, and they are the spiritualists.' They were also known as the people of the pillar. The author of al-Asfar al-Arba'a refers to them saying 'He wanted to gather the sayings of the Peripatetics and the purity of the people of illumination from the Stoic philosophers.' The author of Dustur al-'Ulama' divides the followers of Plato into three sects, among them 'the Stoics, who attended his council and sat in the portico and took the lights of wisdom from his expressions and indications.' And although Muslims knew the name of Stoicism – as we have seen – and the names of some Stoics, we do not find in the lists of translated books any mention of the books of Zeno or Chrysippus or other Stoics. This matter is made more difficult by the fact that the Stoic books themselves did not reach us in the modern world – rather, they have all been lost – and historians of philosophy have taken contradictory positions on this logic. While Zeller, Prantl, and Hamelin deny any originality to this logic, Brochard attempts to prove its originality and independence.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a historical survey of Muslim knowledge of the Stoic school. The author begins by noting that Muslim historians of philosophy did mention the Stoics. He cites three historians: al-Shahrastani, al-Qifti, and the author of Asfar al-Arba'a (likely Mulla Sadra), and the author of Dustur al-'Ulama'. Each provides different names and descriptions: al-Shahrastani calls them 'philosophers of the tents' with Chrysippus and Zeno; al-Qifti reports a classification by Hunayn and Farabi mentioning a sect called the people of the portico; the author of Asfar al-Arba'a quotes a reference to gathering the purity of the people of illumination from Stoic philosophers; Dustur al-'Ulama' divides Plato's followers including Stoics. The author then notes a paradox: although Muslims knew the name and some individuals of Stoicism, there are no translations of their actual books. This difficulty is compounded by the fact that all Stoic originals are lost in the modern world. Modern historians disagree: Zeller, Prantl, and Hamelin deny originality to Stoic logic, while Brochard affirms it. This sets the stage for the author's investigation of Stoic elements in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
الرواقيون
أصحاب المظلة
خروسيس
زينون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what conclusion can be drawn about Muslim knowledge of Stoicism?
A) They had full access to Stoic texts and doctrines.
B) They knew the name and some individuals but lacked translated books.
C) They did not know Stoicism at all.
D) They translated all Stoic works into Arabic but later lost them.

Key Takeaway
The presence of a school name in Islamic sources does not prove access to its original texts; historians must verify textual transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Five Channels of Stoic Influence on Islamic Logic
Author enumerates five channels through which Stoic ideas entered Islamic logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and trace the five historical channels through which Stoic ideas entered Islamic logic, including indirect transmission through critics.

Core Idea
Stoic influence on Islamic logic came through translation, Christian monks, Sabians, Greek commentators, and Gnostic sects.

Stoic elements are found throughout Islamic logical tradition, but how did they get there? The author provides a clear enumeration of five channels, from early translations to contact with sectarian groups. Understanding these channels helps you see that Islamic logic is not purely Aristotelian but absorbed influences from competing schools of thought. Pay attention to the irony that even critics of Stoicism transmitted Stoic ideas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five sources
List the five sources mentioned: (1) translation of Stoic books in the Umayyad period, (2) contact with Christian monks in monasteries, (3) Sabians of Harran and Hermetic writings, (4) later Greek commentators of the Organon (Alexander of Aphrodisias and Galen), (5) Daytsaniyya churches.
Each source represents a different mode of transmission: direct translation, cultural contact, religious sects, scholarly commentaries, and community traditions.
2
Classify the type of transmission for each
Determine for each source whether the transmission was direct (e.g., translation) or indirect (e.g., through refutations). For example, Alexander and Galen attacked Stoicism yet their commentaries still contained Stoic elements, so their transmission was indirect.
This shows that ideas can spread even through opposition.
3
Connect to Islamic thinkers mentioned
The passage specifically links the Daytsaniyya to the nominalist tendency of Hisham ibn al-Hakam and al-Nazzam al-Mu'tazili. Consider how Stoic nominalism might have influenced their theological positions on universals.
Hisham and al-Nazzam were early Mu'tazilites, so this shows Stoic influence on early Kalam.
4
Recognize the irony in the Galen example
Galen wrote a critique of the Stoics (called 'people of the tent'), yet his works were a source of Stoic ideas. Reflect on how even hostile sources can transmit doctrines.
This highlights the importance of examining all sources, not just sympathetic ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسنرى في خلال هذا البحث عناصر رواقية في منطق الشراح والملخصين وفي نقد الأصوليين والفقهاء الهدمي لمنطق أرسطو ولكن إذا كانت هناك عناصر رواقية في التراث المنطقي الإسلامي فينبغي تحديد المصادر التي استمد منها المسلمون هذه العناصر . وتتلخص تلك المصادر فيما يأتي . أولاً : من المرجح أن يكون بعض الكتب الرواقية نقلت في العصر الأموي . ثانياً : عن طريق الاحتكاك العلمي والاتصال بالقسس في الأديرة والكنائس وللرواقية أثر كبير على هذه المدارس . ثالثاً : عن طريق صائبة حران ، وهي فرقة أفلاطونية ودخل منها أيضاً عناصر رواقية . كما أن الكتابات الهرميسية - وهي في مجموعة أفلاطونية محدثة ممتزجة بعقائد الحرمين تحوي عدداً كبيراً من الآراء الرواقية . رابعاً : عن طريق المتأخرين من شراح الأورجانون اليونان - وقد نقلت هذه الشروح إلى العالم الإسلامي وفيها عناصر رواقية كثيرة ، وأهم الشراح اليونان الاسكندر الافروديسي وجالينوس - ومن المعروف أن هذين المفكرين هاجما الرواقية . خامساً : كانت الكنائس الديصانية موطناً للآراء الرواقية وقد أثرت في النزعة الإسمية لدى هشام بن الحكم والنظام المعتزلي . ويذكر العرب عن جالينوس أنه كتب في نقد أصحاب المظلة وأصحاب المظلة هم الرواقيون .
And we shall see in the course of this research Stoic elements in the logic of the commentators and summarizers and in the criticism of the Ash'ari theologians and jurists of Aristotle's logic. But if there are Stoic elements in the Islamic logical heritage, then the sources from which Muslims derived these elements must be identified. These sources are summarized as follows. First: It is probable that some Stoic books were translated in the Umayyad era. Second: Through scientific interaction and contact with priests in monasteries and churches, and Stoicism had a great influence on these schools. Third: Through the Sabians of Harran, who were a Platonic sect, and Stoic elements also entered from them. Also, the Hermetic writings – which are a collection of late Platonism mixed with the beliefs of the two sanctuaries – contain a large number of Stoic opinions. Fourth: Through the later commentators of the Greek Organon – these commentaries were translated into the Islamic world and contain many Stoic elements, the most important Greek commentators being Alexander of Aphrodisias and Galen – and it is known that these two thinkers attacked Stoicism. Fifth: The Daytsaniyya churches were a home for Stoic opinions and influenced the nominalist tendency of Hisham ibn al-Hakam and al-Nazzam al-Mu'tazili. The Arabs mention about Galen that he wrote a critique of the people of the tent, and the people of the tent are the Stoics.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage lists five sources of Stoic influence in Islamic logic. The author notes that in his research he will identify Stoic elements in the logic of the commentators (e.g., Ibn Sina's followers) and in the criticisms of Ash'ari theologians and jurists against Aristotle. He then lists the sources: first, translation of Stoic books in the Umayyad period. Second, contact with Christian monks – monasteries were influenced by Stoicism. Third, the Sabians of Harran, a Platonic sect, and the Hermetic writings (often associated with Hermes Trismegistus) which contain Stoic ideas. Fourth, later Greek commentators like Alexander of Aphrodisias and Galen – these commentaries were translated and contained Stoic elements, even though both Alexander and Galen were critics of Stoicism. Fifth, the Daytsaniyya churches (followers of Bardaisan) which were centers of Stoic thought and influenced the nominalist leanings of Hisham ibn al-Hakam and al-Nazzam, early Mu'tazilites. Finally, he notes that Galen himself wrote a critique of the Stoics, indicating that knowledge of Stoicism came through refutations as well.

Key Terms
صائبة حران
الهرميسية
الديصانية
النزعة الإسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Plato and Aristotle
B) Alexander of Aphrodisias and Galen
C) Zeno and Chrysippus
D) Al-Farabi and Ibn Sina

Key Takeaway
Stoic influence on Islamic logic was indirect, coming through multiple intermediaries including Christian monasteries, Sabian pagans, and even critics of Stoicism like Galen.

Muslim Awareness of Skeptic Arguments
Author notes that Muslims knew Skeptic arguments widely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how Muslim theologians and philosophers were aware of and engaged with Skeptic arguments.

Core Idea
Muslim scholars knew Skeptic arguments thoroughly.

This passage states that no work of kalam or philosophy is without Skeptic arguments. Understanding this widespread engagement helps us see how Islamic thought confronted skepticism head-on. Let's examine the claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
Read the first sentence: 'As for the Skeptics, the Muslims knew their arguments.' The author asserts familiarity, not just awareness.
Note the emphasis on 'every book of kalam or philosophy'.
2
Consider the scope of this claim
The author says it is futile to list specific books because Skeptic ideas appear universally in Islamic theological and philosophical works.
This suggests deep integration, not isolated references.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does this matter? It shows that Islamic thinkers engaged with the strongest challenges to certainty, which sharpened their own arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن الشكاك فعرف المسلمون حججهم ، ولا يخلو كتاب من كتب الكلام أو الفلسفة من عرض لآرائهم بحيث يكون من العبث محاولة تعيين الكتب التي ذكرت آراءهم . ( يعبر الطوسي عن السوفسطائية بالعندية والعنادية وباللاادرية عن الشكاك ) ولكن هناك مسألة على جانب كبير من الأهمية : هل نقلت كتبهم نفسها إلى العربية أم أن آراءهم نقلت عن طريق غير مباشر .
As for the Skeptics, the Muslims knew their arguments, and no book of theology or philosophy is devoid of presenting their opinions, to the extent that it would be futile to try to identify the books that mentioned their opinions. (Al-Tusi expresses the Sophists as 'the obstinate' and the Skeptics as 'the non-knowers'.) But there is a question of great importance: Were their books themselves translated into Arabic, or were their opinions transmitted indirectly?
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage addresses the Skeptics (الشكاك). The author states that Muslims were well aware of Skeptic arguments; every work of theology (kalam) or philosophy includes them. He notes that al-Tusi (Nasir al-Din al-Tusi) expressed the Sophists as 'al-'indiyya wa al-'anadiyya' (those who refer to themselves or are obstinate) and the Skeptics as 'al-la adriyya' (those who say 'I don't know'). The author then raises a key historical question: were the Skeptic texts themselves translated into Arabic, or were their ideas transmitted indirectly through other works? This question is significant for understanding the depth of Skeptical influence.

Key Terms
الشكاك
السوفسطائية
العندية والعنادية
اللاادرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Through every book of kalam or philosophy presenting their opinions.
C) Only through al-Tusi's writings.
D) They did not know the arguments at all.

Key Takeaway
Muslim scholars had comprehensive knowledge of Skeptic arguments, as evidenced by their ubiquity in kalam and philosophical works.

Al-Tusi's Terminology for Skeptics and Sophists
Author notes al-Tusi's terminology for Sophists and Skeptics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-Tusi's terms for Sophists ('al-'indiyya wa al-'anadiyya') and Skeptics ('al-la adriyya') and explain their significance.

Core Idea
Al-Tusi coined precise Arabic terms to differentiate Sophists from Skeptics.

In a parenthetical remark, the author mentions al-Tusi's unique terminology. Understanding these terms is important for reading later Islamic texts accurately. Let's unpack what each term means and why the distinction matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups and their terms
Al-Tusi calls the Sophists 'al-'indiyya wa al-'anadiyya' and the Skeptics 'al-la adriyya'. Note the Arabic roots.
'La adri' means 'I do not know' — fitting for skeptics.
2
Understand the meaning of each term
'Al-'indiyya' likely refers to 'those who refer to themselves' (from 'inda = at/by), and 'al-'anadiyya' to 'the obstinate'. These capture aspects of sophistry: self-reference and contentiousness. 'Al-la adriyya' literally means 'the I-don't-know-ists'.
3
Consider why al-Tusi created these terms
The author implies that previous parlance may have conflated Sophists and Skeptics. Al-Tusi's terms clarify that skeptics doubt knowledge, while sophists argue fallaciously for the sake of argument.
This precision helps later scholars target their critiques accurately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن الشكاك فعرف المسلمون حججهم ، ولا يخلو كتاب من كتب الكلام أو الفلسفة من عرض لآرائهم بحيث يكون من العبث محاولة تعيين الكتب التي ذكرت آراءهم . ( يعبر الطوسي عن السوفسطائية بالعندية والعنادية وباللاادرية عن الشكاك ) ولكن هناك مسألة على جانب كبير من الأهمية : هل نقلت كتبهم نفسها إلى العربية أم أن آراءهم نقلت عن طريق غير مباشر .
As for the Skeptics, the Muslims knew their arguments, and no book of theology or philosophy is devoid of presenting their opinions, to the extent that it would be futile to try to identify the books that mentioned their opinions. (Al-Tusi expresses the Sophists as 'the obstinate' and the Skeptics as 'the non-knowers'.) But there is a question of great importance: Were their books themselves translated into Arabic, or were their opinions transmitted indirectly?
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage addresses the Skeptics (الشكاك). The author states that Muslims were well aware of Skeptic arguments; every work of theology (kalam) or philosophy includes them. He notes that al-Tusi (Nasir al-Din al-Tusi) expressed the Sophists as 'al-'indiyya wa al-'anadiyya' (those who refer to themselves or are obstinate) and the Skeptics as 'al-la adriyya' (those who say 'I don't know'). The author then raises a key historical question: were the Skeptic texts themselves translated into Arabic, or were their ideas transmitted indirectly through other works? This question is significant for understanding the depth of Skeptical influence.

Evolution of the Organon's Contents
Author traces changes in the books included in the logical Organon across traditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the historical evolution of the Organon's contents and explain how the Stoic reduction shaped the formalist conception of logic.

Core Idea
Logic's scope expanded and contracted across traditions — the battle over its material content.

Today's lesson examines how the very definition of logic shifted as the books included in the Organon changed. You will see that for Aristotle, logic included formal and material aspects, but later Stoic-influenced logicians stripped away the material books, leaving only the pure form. Understanding this evolution reveals a fundamental disagreement: is logic only about form, or does it also deal with the subject matter of arguments?

Guided Path
1
Aristotle's original list
Identify the six books Aristotle included in the Organon: Categories, De Interpretatione, Prior Analytics, Posterior Analytics, Topics, and Sophistics.
These cover both formal structure (Prior Analytics) and material content (Posterior Analytics, Topics, Sophistics).
2
Additions by Greek and Alexandrian commentators
Note that Greek commentators added Rhetoric and Poetics, and Alexandrian commentators added Porphyry's Isagoge as an introduction. This expanded the Organon to nine books.
The Isagoge became standard in later traditions.
3
Islamic Peripatetic reception
Recognize that Islamic Peripatetic logicians like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina followed the Alexandrian order, including all nine books.
This shows the influence of the commentary tradition.
4
Stoic-influenced deletion
Understand that later Stoic-oriented logicians deleted the books dealing with material logic: Posterior Analytics, Topics, Rhetoric, Poetics, and Sophistics, leaving only the formal books.
They argued logic should be purely formal, not concerned with subject matter.
5
Formal vs. material logic
Reflect on the difference: formal logic studies argument structure, while material logic evaluates the truth and demonstrative power of premises. The deletion reflects a purely formal view.
This debate continues in Islamic philosophy and usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة : ويتصل بصورية المنطق ومادته مسألة محتويات الأورجانون وأقسامه ، فقد كان الأورجانون يشمل عند أرسطو المقولات والعبارة والتحليلات الأولى والتحليلات الثانية والجدل والسفسطة . ولكن شراح أرسطو اليونانيين أضافوا اليها الشعر والخطابة . ووضع المتأخرون من الشراح الإسكندريين إيساغوجي مقدمة لهذه الكتب ، وتابعهم الشراح المشاؤون الإسلاميون . غير أن المتأخرين من المناطقة أو الشراح الرواقيين لم يأخذوا بهذا الرأي ، بل كان لهم رأي يخالف الإثنين ويتفق مع نظرتهم العامة عن المنطق ـ المذهب الصوري البحت ـ فحذفوا كما قلنا التحليلات الثانية والجدل والخطابة والشعر والسفسطة وهي التي تبحث في مادة المنطق . ويبدو الأثر الرواقي هنا واضحاً تمام الوضوح .
The third issue: Connected to the formality and materiality of logic is the issue of the contents of the Organon and its divisions. The Organon in Aristotle included Categories, De Interpretatione, Prior Analytics, Posterior Analytics, Topics, and Sophistics. But the Greek commentators of Aristotle added Poetry and Rhetoric. The later Alexandrian commentators placed Porphyry's Isagoge as an introduction to these books, and the Islamic Peripatetic commentators followed them. However, the later logicians or Stoic commentators did not adopt this view; rather, they had an opinion opposing both and consistent with their general view of logic – the purely formal school – so they deleted, as we said, the Posterior Analytics, Topics, Rhetoric, Poetry, and Sophistics, which treat the matter of logic. The Stoic influence here is completely clear.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage addresses the changing composition of the logical corpus. Aristotle's Organon had six books, but Greek commentators added Rhetoric and Poetry, and Alexandrian commentators added Isagoge as an introduction. The Islamic Peripatetics followed that. However, the Stoic-oriented later logicians removed the books dealing with the material of logic (Posterior Analytics, Topics, Rhetoric, Poetry, Sophistics), leaving only the formal parts. This demonstrates the Stoic influence towards purely formal logic.

Key Takeaway
The Organon's contents are not fixed; they reflect competing views on whether logic should include the material dimension of arguments.

Abu al-Barakat's Correction on Conditionals
Historical-critical analysis of conditional propositions' origin.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Abu al-Barakat revealed that conditionals were not from Aristotle but from later traditions.

This passage traces how Islamic logicians came to realize that Aristotle did not originate conditional propositions. It explains the transmission from Theophrastus and the Stoics, and highlights the critical insight of Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, who corrected a longstanding error.

Guided Path

Locate where Ibn Sina and later commentators attributed a detailed theory of conditionals to Aristotle. Note that this claim was made without evidence.
Ibn Sina was influenced by later Greek commentators.

الفَصْلُ الثَّالِثُ مبحث التصديقات عرضنا في الفصل السابق مباحث التصورات - وسنعرض في هذا الفصل مباحث التصديقات - وهي تتكون من مبحثين - مبحث القضايا ومبحث الاستدلال - والصلة بين المبحثين صلة ظاهرة لا تحتاج إلى توضيح . أما القضايا فخصص أرسطو لبحثها كتاباً من كتبه المنطقية وهو « العبارة » وتناول الإسلاميون هذا الكتاب بالشرح والتفصيل ولم يقفوا عند أبحاث أرسطو بل أضافوا إليها أبحاثاً أخرى . وأول وأهم تلك الابحاث التي أضافها الإسلاميون - حملية كانت أو شرطية في متناول الجميع في الكتب الكثيرة بحيث لا نجد مطلقاً أي داع لاعادة شرحها ، ولكننا سنوضح فقط نقطة واحدة - هي هل عرف الإسلاميون أن أرسطو لم يضع هذه القضايا . أما غالبية الشراح الإسلاميين فذكروا هذه القضايا كما ذكروا مباحث المنطق غير الأرسططاليسية بدون أن ينسبوها إلى أصحابها الأصليين . وذكر بعض المناطقة ، ومنهم ابن سينا - متأثرين بالشراح اليونانيين المتأخرين - أن لأرسطو في القضايا الشرطية نظرية مفصلة لم تصل إلى أيديهم مصادرها(١) وتابع هذا الرأي أغلب الشراح الاسلاميين ولكن منطقياً جاء بعد ابن سينا ـ وهو أبو البركات البغدادي ـ تنبه إلى أن أرسطو لم يذكر القضايا الشرطية في كتبه . ويعلل أبو البركات إهمال أرسطو لها أما لقلة فائدتها في العلوم فلم يشأ التطويل بها وإما لأنه اعتمد على أن الأذهان التي عرفت الحمليات تنتهي منها إليها فتعرفها بما عرفته من الحمليات(٢) . هذا تحليل بارع للمسألة ، يصحح به أبو البركات الخطأ الذي وقع فيه شراح الاسكندرية المتأخرون . كما وقع فيه الإسلاميون من بعد . ولا يكتفي أبو البركات بهذا ، بل يذكر أن بعض المتأخرين يعتقد أن أرسطو صنف في القضايا الشرطية كتاباً خاصاً لم ينقل إلى العربية، ويقرر أبو البركات أن هذا تخمين باطل لأن أرسطو لو أراد ذكرها لألحقها بمباحث العبارة ولما أتعب نفسه في افراد كتاب خاص بها ، علاوة على أن هذه القضايا ليس فيها ما يستحق أن يكون موضوعاً لكتاب خاص(٣) . وهذه النتيجة يصل إليها صاحب شرح حكمة الإشراق فيقول : « إن أرسطو أهمل في كتبه المتصلات والمنفصلات والاقترانات الشرطية التي زادها المتأخرون لأنه لا ينتفع بها في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة »(٤) ، والغريب أن الصدر الشيرازي يخالف صاحب المتن نفسه ـ السهروردي في نظرته إلى هذه القضايا ـ فان السهروردي عالج هذه القضايا قبل معالجته للقضايا الحملية . ومن الواضح أن السهروردي متأثر تماماً بالرواقية وقد أهمل ذكر القضايا الشرطية بعض المفكرين الذين تأثروا في بعض مباحثهم المنطقية الأخرى بالرواقية ، ومن هؤلاء إخوان الصفا والخوارزمي . القسم الثاني من التصديقات : هو مباحث الإستدلال ويشمل هذا
The Third Chapter: The Topic of Assertions. We presented in the previous chapter the topics of concepts, and we will present in this chapter the topics of assertions. It consists of two topics: the topic of propositions and the topic of inference, and the connection between the two topics is obvious and needs no clarification. As for propositions, Aristotle devoted a book of his logical works to them, namely 'De Interpretatione', and the Islamic scholars dealt with this book with commentary and detail, and they did not stop at Aristotle's researches but added other researches to them. The first and most important of those additions made by the Islamic scholars – following the Greek commentators – is the division of propositions into categorical and conditional, and the division of the conditional into conjunctive and disjunctive. Aristotelian logic did not know conditional propositions at all in their two types. But they first appeared in Theophrastus and Eudemus. Then they appeared in their complete form among the Stoics, and through the Greek commentators they were transmitted to the Islamic world. The topics of these propositions – whether categorical or conditional – are available to everyone in many books, such that we find absolutely no need to explain them again. However, we will clarify only one point: Did the Islamic scholars know that Aristotle did not set down these propositions? Most of the Islamic commentators mentioned these propositions as they mentioned non-Aristotelian logical topics without attributing them to their original authors. Some logicians, including Ibn Sina – influenced by the later Greek commentators – mentioned that Aristotle had a detailed theory of conditional propositions whose sources did not reach them. (1) Most of the Islamic commentators followed this view. But a logician came after Ibn Sina – namely Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi – who noticed that Aristotle did not mention conditional propositions in his books. Abu al-Barakat explains Aristotle's omission either because of their lack of benefit in the sciences, so he did not wish to elaborate on them, or because he relied on the fact that minds that knew categoricals would infer them from what they knew of categoricals (2). This is an ingenious analysis of the issue, by which Abu al-Barakat corrects the error that later Alexandrian commentators fell into, as did the subsequent Islamic scholars. Abu al-Barakat does not stop at this, but mentions that some later scholars believe that Aristotle composed a special book on conditional propositions that was not translated into Arabic. Abu al-Barakat declares that this is a false conjecture, because if Aristotle had wanted to mention them, he would have appended them to the topics of De Interpretatione and would not have troubled himself to devote a special book to them, especially since these propositions do not contain anything worthy of being the subject of a special book (3). The author of Sharh Hikmat al-Ishraq reaches this conclusion, saying: 'Aristotle neglected in his books the conjunctive, disjunctive, and conditional syllogisms that later scholars added, because they are of no benefit in this world or the next' (4). It is strange that al-Sadr al-Shirazi disagrees with the author of the text itself – al-Suhrawardi – in his view of these propositions, for al-Suhrawardi treated these propositions before his treatment of categorical propositions. It is clear that al-Suhrawardi is completely influenced by the Stoics. Some thinkers who were influenced by the Stoics in some of their other logical inquiries neglected to mention conditional propositions, among them the Ikhwan al-Safa and al-Khwarizmi. The second part of assertions: it is the topics of inference, and this includes...
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a historical-critical discussion of the origin of conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya) in Islamic logic. It opens by introducing the third chapter on assertions (tasdiqat), noting that it comprises two sections: propositions and inference. The author then focuses on propositions, stating that Aristotle wrote a book on them (De Interpretatione), but Islamic scholars expanded it. The key addition by Islamic scholars, following Greek commentators, was the division of propositions into categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya), and the further division of conditionals into conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). The author asserts that Aristotelian logic did not know conditional propositions; they first appeared with Theophrastus and Eudemus, then fully with the Stoics, and were transmitted to Islam via Greek commentators. The author then raises the question: Did Islamic scholars realize that Aristotle did not originate these propositions? Most commentators mentioned them without attribution, but some, like Ibn Sina, claimed Aristotle had a detailed theory. Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi corrected this, arguing that Aristotle deliberately omitted them either because they were not beneficial or because he deemed them derivable from categoricals. Abu al-Barakat also refutes the claim that Aristotle wrote a separate book on conditionals, calling it baseless. The author then quotes the commentator on Hikmat al-Ishraq, who says Aristotle neglected them as useless. The passage ends by noting that al-Suhrawardi treated conditionals before categoricals, showing Stoic influence, while others like Ikhwan al-Safa and al-Khwarizmi omitted them. The lesson closes with a transition to the second part on inference.

Abu al-Barakat's Correction on Aristotle and Conditionals
Critique of the belief that Aristotle discussed conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Abu al-Barakat's correction regarding Aristotle's treatment of conditional propositions and evaluate its significance in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Aristotle did not discuss conditional propositions; Abu al-Barakat corrected centuries of misunderstanding.

Many early Islamic logicians believed Aristotle had a detailed theory of conditional propositions, but Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi noticed something crucial: Aristotle never actually mentioned them in his surviving works. This lesson unpacks Abu al-Barakat's insightful analysis and shows how it corrected a widespread error among commentators. You will learn to distinguish historical fact from conjecture and appreciate the critical role of textual evidence in logical historiography.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common claim about Aristotle
Read the opening of the Arabic passage. The author states that most Islamic and Alexandrian commentators believed Aristotle had a detailed theory of conditional propositions whose sources were lost. Recognize this as the target of correction.
This belief was widespread for centuries.
2
Observe Abu al-Barakat's key observation
Abu al-Barakat noted that Aristotle did not mention conditional propositions in his books at all. This is the starting point of his correction. Verify this by thinking about Aristotle's known works like the Categories and De Interpretatione.
Contrast with the commentators' assumption.
3
Understand the two explanations for omission
Abu al-Barakat offers two reasons: either Aristotle considered conditionals of little use and thus omitted them, or he believed that minds already familiar with categoricals would derive conditionals naturally. Reflect on the plausibility of each.
These are complementary, not mutually exclusive.
4
Evaluate the refutation of the 'lost book' theory
Some later scholars claimed Aristotle wrote a separate book on conditionals that was not translated into Arabic. Abu al-Barakat rejects this as false conjecture, arguing that if Aristotle intended to discuss them, he would have appended them to De Interpretatione rather than compose a new work. Assess the strength of this argument.
This is a logical argument from textual evidence.
5
Connect to the broader Islamic tradition
The passage notes that al-Suhrawardi and the Ikhwan al-Safa also omitted conditionals, while Sadr al-Din al-Shirazi disagreed with al-Suhrawardi. Consider how this historical debate shaped later Islamic logic, especially in integrating conditionals.
The correction did not end the debate but clarified the historical record.

لأرسطو في القضايا الشرطية نظرية مفصلة لم تصل إلى أيديهم مصادرها(١) وتابع هذا الرأي أغلب الشراح الاسلاميين ولكن منطقياً جاء بعد ابن سينا ـ وهو أبو البركات البغدادي ـ تنبه إلى أن أرسطو لم يذكر القضايا الشرطية في كتبه . ويعلل أبو البركات إهمال أرسطو لها أما لقلة فائدتها في العلوم فلم يشأ التطويل بها وإما لأنه اعتمد على أن الأذهان التي عرفت الحمليات تنتهي منها إليها فتعرفها بما عرفته من الحمليات(٢) . هذا تحليل بارع للمسألة ، يصحح به أبو البركات الخطأ الذي وقع فيه شراح الاسكندرية المتأخرون . كما وقع فيه الإسلاميون من بعد . ولا يكتفي أبو البركات بهذا ، بل يذكر أن بعض المتأخرين يعتقد أن أرسطو صنف في القضايا الشرطية كتاباً خاصاً لم ينقل إلى العربية، ويقرر أبو البركات أن هذا تخمين باطل لأن أرسطو لو أراد ذكرها لألحقها بمباحث العبارة ولما أتعب نفسه في افراد كتاب خاص بها ، علاوة على أن هذه القضايا ليس فيها ما يستحق أن يكون موضوعاً لكتاب خاص(٣) . وهذه النتيجة يصل إليها صاحب شرح حكمة الإشراق فيقول : « إن أرسطو أهمل في كتبه المتصلات والمنفصلات والاقترانات الشرطية التي زادها المتأخرون لأنه لا ينتفع بها في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة »(٤) ، والغريب أن الصدر الشيرازي يخالف صاحب المتن نفسه ـ السهروردي في نظرته إلى هذه القضايا ـ فان السهروردي عالج هذه القضايا قبل معالجته للقضايا الحملية . ومن الواضح أن السهروردي متأثر تماماً بالرواقية وقد أهمل ذكر القضايا الشرطية بعض المفكرين الذين تأثروا في بعض مباحثهم المنطقية الأخرى بالرواقية ، ومن هؤلاء إخوان الصفا والخوارزمي .
Aristotle had a detailed theory of conditional propositions whose sources did not reach them(1). Most Islamic commentators followed this view, but a logician after Ibn Sina—namely Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi—noticed that Aristotle did not mention conditional propositions in his books. Abu al-Barakat explains Aristotle's omission either because of their little benefit in the sciences, so he did not wish to elaborate on them, or because he relied on the fact that minds that have understood categorical propositions arrive at them through those categorical propositions(2). This is an insightful analysis by which Abu al-Barakat corrects the error committed by the later Alexandrian commentators, and also by later Muslims. Abu al-Barakat does not stop here, but mentions that some later scholars believe that Aristotle composed a special book on conditional propositions that was not translated into Arabic. Abu al-Barakat declares this to be a false conjecture, because if Aristotle had wanted to mention them, he would have appended them to the topics of 'Interpretation' (De Interpretatione) and would not have troubled himself to produce a special book on them, especially since these propositions do not deserve to be the subject of a special book(3). The author of Sharh Hikmat al-Ishraq reaches the same conclusion, saying: 'Aristotle neglected in his books the conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals and the conditional syllogisms that later scholars added, because they are of no use in this world or the next'(4). Strangely, Sadr al-Din al-Shirazi contradicts the author of the text himself—al-Suhrawardi—in his view on these propositions, for al-Suhrawardi treated these propositions before treating categorical propositions. It is clear that al-Suhrawardi was completely influenced by Stoicism, and some thinkers who were influenced in some of their other logical discussions by Stoicism also neglected to mention conditional propositions, among them the Ikhwan al-Safa and al-Khwarizmi.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a critical historical analysis. The author begins by stating that many commentators believed Aristotle had a detailed theory of conditional propositions, but that the original sources were lost. Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, however, observed that Aristotle never actually mentioned conditional propositions in his extant works. Abu al-Barakat offers two possible explanations for this silence: either Aristotle considered conditional propositions of little use and thus omitted them, or he assumed that anyone who understood categorical propositions could derive conditionals from them without separate treatment. The author praises this as a perceptive correction of earlier commentators, including the Alexandrian school and later Islamic thinkers. Abu al-Barakat further refutes the claim that Aristotle wrote a separate book on conditionals that was lost, arguing that if Aristotle intended to discuss them, he would have included them in his work 'On Interpretation' rather than writing an independent treatise. This view is echoed by the author of Sharh Hikmat al-Ishraq, who dismisses conditionals as useless. The passage also notes a deviation: while al-Suhrawardi himself treated conditionals before categoricals, Sadr al-Din al-Shirazi disagreed with him. Finally, the author points out that some philosophers influenced by Stoicism, such as the Ikhwan al-Safa and al-Khwarizmi, also omitted conditionals. The lesson highlights the historical debate over Aristotle's treatment of conditionals and the critical role of Abu al-Barakat in correcting misunderstandings.

The Dual Heritage of Islamic Logic: Stoic and Peripatetic
Author concludes that Islamic logic synthesizes Stoic and Peripatetic trends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two major influences on Islamic logic (Stoic and Peripatetic) and explain how the author refutes the notion that it is purely Aristotelian.

Core Idea
Islamic logic is not a copy of Aristotle—it fuses Stoic and Peripatetic traditions.

Many assume Islamic logic is simply Aristotle translated into Arabic. This passage concludes the author's argument that this view is mistaken. Based on three chapters of analysis, he identifies two distinct trends that shaped the logic of the Islamic commentators: a Stoic trend and a Peripatetic trend. Understanding this dual heritage helps us appreciate the unique character of Islamic logical works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common assumption
The author begins by noting that Islamic logic is commonly thought to be purely Aristotelian. Find where he states this in the passage.
Look for the phrase 'ليس كما يظن منطقاً أرسططاليسياً بحتاً'.
2
Extract the author's thesis
The author claims two trends dominated Islamic logic. Identify both and note their names in Arabic and English.
The two trends are 'رواقي' (Stoic) and 'مشائي' (Peripatetic).
3
Analyze the evidence base
The author refers to 'three chapters' as the basis for his conclusion. Consider that the book has provided detailed comparisons. What kinds of features might belong to each trend?
Stoic logic emphasizes propositional logic and conditional syllogisms; Peripatetic focuses on categorical syllogisms.
4
Contrast the Stoic trend
The author says the Stoic trend opposes Aristotelian logic in its general features and characteristics. How does this refine the common perception of Islamic logic?
It suggests that many elements of Islamic logic are non-Aristotelian, stemming from Stoic sources.

بهذا تنتهي طرق الاستدلال عند الاسلاميين أو بمعنى أدق تنتهي من منطق الشراح الإسلاميين نفسه، وقد رأينا في الفصول الثلاثة التي بحثنا فيها هذا المنطق - أن هذا المنطق ليس كما يظن منطقاً أرسططاليسياً بحتاً ـ ولكن سيطرة عليه اتجاهان : اتجاه رواقي واتجاه مشائي . أما الاتجاه الأول ، فقد رأيناه يخالف المنطق الأرسططاليسي في مميزاته العامة وخصائصه . كما رأينا أن هذه المميزات العامة تسيطر على عناصره .
With this, the deductive paths among Muslims end, or more precisely, they end from the logic of the Islamic commentators themselves. We have seen in the three chapters in which we discussed this logic that this logic is not, as is thought, purely Aristotelian. Rather, two trends dominated it: a Stoic trend and a Peripatetic trend. As for the first trend, we have seen it opposing Aristotelian logic in its general features and characteristics. We have also seen that these general features dominate its elements.

Commentary
This concluding passage summarizes the author's thesis that the logic of the Islamic commentators (shurrah) is not purely Aristotelian but is influenced by two trends: a Stoic trend (ittijah Riwaqi) and a Peripatetic trend (ittijah Masha'i). The Stoic trend in particular is said to oppose Aristotelian logic in its general features and characteristics, and these features dominate many elements of Islamic logic. This refines the common perception of Islamic logic as merely a transmission of Aristotle.

Read a passage from al-Ghazali's 'Mi'yar al-'Ilm' and identify one element that shows Stoic influence (e.g., a conditional syllogism) and one that shows Peripatetic influence (e.g., a categorical syllogism).

Refuting the Claim of Early Theologians' Use of Aristotelian Logic
Author refutes orientalist claim about early theologians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three arguments that falsely support the claim that early theologians used Aristotelian logic, and reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's counter-claim.

Core Idea
Early theologians rejected Aristotelian logic; al-Ghazali later introduced it.

This lesson examines a common claim among orientalists that early mutakallimun used Aristotelian logic in their debates. The author refutes this by presenting three arguments that appear to support the claim but are ultimately flawed, and then provides positive evidence from Ibn Taymiyya that all early theological schools opposed Aristotelian logic. Understanding this critique is crucial for correctly periodizing the integration of Greek logic into Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the targeted claim
Find the exact claim that the author is critiquing. It is that early theologians (mutakallimun up to the 5th century AH) used Aristotelian logic in their theological debates.
2
List the three supporting arguments
The author mentions three reasons that might have led to this false idea: (1) Mu'tazila debates with Christians who used Greek logic, (2) lack of surviving Mu'tazila books to verify their methodology, (3) assumption that later theologians' style was also used by earlier ones.
These are not endorsed by the author; they are presented as the basis of the misconception.
3
Analyse each argument's weakness
For each argument, consider why it is insufficient. The author implies that none of these establish that early theologians actually used Aristotelian logic; they only make it plausible in a shallow sense.
The author does not elaborate on the weaknesses but considers the claim 'completely corrupt'.
4
Find the counter-evidence
The author uses Ibn Taymiyya's explicit statement that all early sects (Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, Shi'a, Karamiyya) criticized Aristotelian logic and paid no attention to it, and that al-Ghazali was the first to mix it with Islamic principles.
This is the key refutation: direct testimony from an authority.
5
Assess the historical shift
Note that the author acknowledges that later theologians (post-5th century) did use Aristotelian logic. The turning point is al-Ghazali, which defines a clear before and after.
This helps in understanding the periodization of Islamic intellectual history.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفَصْلُ الثَّانِي
موقف علماء أصول الدين - المتكلمين - من المنطق الارسططاليسي حتى القرن الخامس
بحثنا في الفصل السابق موقف علماء أصول الفقه من المنطق الأرسططاليسي حتى القرن الخامس . ووصلنا إلى أن هؤلاء العلماء لم يقبلوا المنطق الأرسططاليسي وسنحاول في هذا الفصل أن نبحث موقف علماء أصول الدين من هذا المنطق . وأول ما نراه حول هذا الموقف فكرة شائعة لدى مؤرخي الفكر الإسلامي وخاصة المستشرقين : وهي أن المتكلمين الأول كانوا رجال دين استخدموا منطق أرسطو في جدلهم اللاهوتي - ومع أن هذه الفكرة فاسدة تمام الفساد - فانها سادت حتى الآن . وربما عاون على تدعيمها الحجج الآتية : -
أولاً - أن المعتزلة بدأوا حجاجهم الديني مع النصارى في وقت مبكر . وكان قد حل مكان المسيحية الأولى الساذجة، مسيحية أخرى مركبة على أساس المنطق اليوناني - أو بمعنى آخر كان النصارى يتسلحون في جدلهم الديني - كما قلت في المقدمة - بالمنطق الأرسطاطاليسي حتى آخر الأشكال الوجودية . ولم يكن مما يقنع هؤلاء النصارى في جدلهم البراهين الشعرية أو الاحتجاجات الخطابية - وهم يتكلمون في اتحاد اللاهوت والناسوت في المسيح وتوحد الأقانيم وتعددها . فكان من المعقول إذن أن لا يبدأ المسلمون هذا النقاش ، وهم خلو من معرفة منطق ارسطو وخاصة أن يوحنا الدمشقي كان يعتبر الإسلام في كتاباته التي يناقش فيها المسلمين ـ كأنه عقيدة نظرية فلسفية .
ثانياً ـ عدم وصول كتب المعتزلة إلى أيدي الباحثين حتى يمكن الحكم القاطع على منهاج البحث لديهم . وكل ما وصل إلينا شذرات مقتضبة في غالب الأحايين عن مذاهبهم أما مناهج البحث لديهم فلم يذكر عنها شيء مطلقاً .
ثالثاً ـ وجود كتب متأخري المتكلمين ـ أي الذين عاشوا بعد القرن الخامس الهجري ـ أدى إلى القول بأن كتب متقدمي المتكلمين ـ أي الذين عاشوا قبل هذا القرن ـ كانت على مثالها . وكتب المتأخرين هذه مؤلفة في معظمها على طريقة المنطق الأرسططاليسي . وقد وصلتنا هذه الكتب ، بينما كتب متكلمي العهد الأول إما مفقودة وإما لم تنشر بعد . ودعا هذا إلى القول بأن علم الكلام تأثر في جميع عصوره بمنطق أرسطو .
وفي الواقع إن المنطق الأرسططاليسي قوبل في المدرسة الكلامية الأولى حتى القرن الخامس أسوأ مقابلة . فهاجمته جميع الفرق الكلامية ، معتزلة أو أشعرية أو شيعة أو كرامية . يقول ابن تيمية : « ما زال نظار المسلمين لا يلتفتون إلى طريقهم بل الأشعرية والمعتزلة والكرامية والشيعة وسائر الطوائف من أهل النظر كانوا يعيبون فسادها . وأول من خلط منطقهم بأصول المسلمين أبو حامد الغزالي » ويقول في فقرة أخرى « ما زال نظار المسلمين بعد أن عرب وعرفوه يعيبونه ويذمونه ولا يلتفتون إليه ولا إلى أهله في موازينهم العقلية والشرعية » . وهذا ما يردده ابن القيم أيضاً.
The Second Chapter: The position of scholars of usul al-din (the theologians) towards Aristotelian logic up to the fifth century. We researched in the previous chapter the position of scholars of usul al-fiqh towards Aristotelian logic up to the fifth century. We concluded that those scholars did not accept Aristotelian logic. We will attempt in this chapter to research the position of scholars of usul al-din towards this logic. The first thing we see regarding this position is a common idea among historians of Islamic thought, especially orientalists: that the early theologians were religious men who used Aristotle's logic in their theological debate - although this idea is completely corrupt - it has prevailed until now. Perhaps the following arguments contributed to its reinforcement: First: The Mu'tazila began their religious debates with the Christians early on. The naive early Christianity had been replaced by another Christianity built upon Greek logic - in other words, the Christians armed themselves in their religious debates - as I stated in the introduction - with Aristotelian logic even to the last existential forms. Poetic proofs or rhetorical arguments would not convince these Christians in their debates - while they discussed the union of divinity and humanity in Christ and the unity and plurality of the hypostases. So it was reasonable that Muslims would not begin this discussion while they were devoid of knowledge of Aristotle's logic, especially since John of Damascus considered Islam in his writings, in which he debated Muslims, as a theoretical philosophical creed. Second: The lack of access to the books of the Mu'tazila by researchers, so that a decisive judgment on their method of inquiry can be made. All that has reached us are brief fragments, mostly about their doctrines, but nothing at all has been mentioned about their methods of inquiry. Third: The existence of books of later theologians - those who lived after the fifth century AH - led to the claim that the books of the earlier theologians - those who lived before this century - were similar. These later books are mostly composed according to Aristotelian logic. These books have reached us, while the books of the first-era theologians are either lost or have not been published yet. This led to the claim that kalam was influenced in all its eras by Aristotle's logic. In reality, Aristotelian logic was met in the early theological school up to the fifth century with the worst reception. All theological sects attacked it, whether Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, Shi'a, or Karamiyya. Ibn Taymiyya says: 'The Muslim theorists never paid attention to their method; rather, the Ash'ariyya, Mu'tazila, Karamiyya, Shi'a, and all other sects of the people of theoretical inquiry used to criticize its corruption. The first to mix their logic with the principles of the Muslims was Abu Hamid al-Ghazali.' In another passage he says: 'The Muslim theorists, after it was translated and they knew it, used to criticize and condemn it, and pay no attention to it or its people in their intellectual and religious balances.' This is also reiterated by Ibn al-Qayyim.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a historical-critical argument. The author begins by stating the chapter's topic: the position of early theologians (mutakallimun) towards Aristotelian logic up to the 5th century AH. After reviewing the stance of usul al-fiqh scholars in the previous chapter, he now turns to usul al-din scholars. The author identifies a prevalent misconception among historians and orientalists: that the early theologians used Aristotelian logic in their theological debates. He declares this idea 'completely corrupt' but notes its persistence. He then presents three arguments that may have reinforced this false notion, though he does not endorse them. First, the early Mu'tazila debated Christians who themselves used Aristotelian logic, making it plausible that Muslims would need to learn that logic to argue effectively. Second, the scarcity of surviving Mu'tazila books prevents researchers from definitively judging their methodology; only scattered fragments about their doctrines remain. Third, later theologians' books (post-5th century) are written in an Aristotelian style, leading to the assumption that earlier works were similar, whereas those earlier works are mostly lost. The author then states the reality: Aristotelian logic was met with severe opposition from all early theological schools (Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, Shi'a, Karamiyya). He supports this with two quotes from Ibn Taymiyya and mentions Ibn al-Qayyim's agreement. The key point is that al-Ghazali was the first to integrate Aristotelian logic with Islamic principles, implying a shift after the 5th century.

المتكلمون
المعتزلة
الأشعرية
أبو حامد الغزالي

Early Theologians' Rejection of Aristotelian Logic
The author argues that early Muslim theologians rejected Aristotelian logic, citing historical evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key evidence used by the author to argue that early Muslim theologians rejected Aristotelian logic, and distinguish this from the later adoption by al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Early Muslim theologians unanimously rejected Aristotelian logic; al-Ghazali was the first to integrate it into Islamic theology.

This lesson examines the historical claim that Islamic theology was always indebted to Aristotelian logic. The passage argues, using historical evidence, that early theologians pre-5th century AH vehemently opposed Greek logic, and that it was only later, starting with al-Ghazali, that it was incorporated. Understanding this historical context is crucial for appreciating the development of Islamic logical thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main thesis
Read the passage to determine the central claim the author is making. Look for the statement that early theologians rejected Aristotelian logic, and note the contrast with later theologians.
The thesis is explicitly stated: 'In reality, Aristotelian logic was met in the first theological school until the fifth century with the worst reception.'
2
Recognize the three reasons for the mistaken belief
The author lists three reasons why scholars erroneously think kalam was always influenced by Aristotle. Identify each reason in the first paragraph.
Reasons: (1) viewing Islam as a philosophical creed, (2) unavailability of early Mu'tazilite works, (3) survival of later works adopting Aristotelian form.
3
Examine the quoted authorities
The author cites Ibn Taymiyya, Ibn al-Qayyim, and Ibn Khaldun. Find what each says about the rejection of Aristotelian logic. Note that Ibn Taymiyya explicitly names al-Ghazali as the first to mix logic with Islamic principles.
Ibn Taymiyya: 'The first to mix their logic with the principles of Muslims was Abu Hamid al-Ghazali.'
4
List the four pieces of evidence
After the quotes, the author provides four numbered pieces of evidence to prove the thesis. Identify each one and how it supports the rejection.
Evidence: (1) different view on definition, (2) rejection of syllogism, (3) analytical criticism of early theologians, (4) existence of critical works.

ومما يثبت هذا إثباتاً واضحاً :
أولاً - اختلاف نظرة المتكلمين والمشائين في مبحث الحد ونقد الأولين لمبحث الحد الأرسططاليسي (١) .
ثانياً - رفض المتكلمين لمبحث القياس الأرسططاليسي . ويذكر لنا ابن خلدون ( ٧٨٥ هـ ١٤٠٦ م ) . « أن المسلمين لم يأخذوا بالأقيسة لملابستها للعلوم الفلسفية المباينة للعقائدة »(٢) ووصلت إلينا مقتطفات من كتاب الآراء والديانات لابن النوبختي الشيعي وفيها نقد للشكل الأول(۳) .
ثالثاً - أظهر لنا النقد التحليلي لنصوص كثيرين من المتكلمين أن الباقلاني (٤) ( ١ صفر سنة ٤٠٣ ) وإمام الحرمين(٥) ، وكثيرين من المعتزلة خرجوا في أبحاثهم عن منطق أرسطو . وصرح أبو سليمان السجستاني بهذا النقد وسيأتي هذا بعد(٦) .
رابعاً - الكتب الكثيرة التي ألفت في نقد المنطق الأرسططاليسي ككتاب الدقائق لأبي بكر بن الطيب والآراء والديانات لإبن النوبختي وتشير نصوص كثيرة إلى أن أبا علي الجبائي وأبا هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار كتبوا في نقد المنطق الأرسططاليسي (٧) . ونقد أبو العباس الناشيء المعتزلي المنطق الأرسططاليسي كما ورد في مناقشة السيرافي ( ٣٦٨ هـ - ٩٧٨ م ) لأبي بشر متى بن يونس ( ٣٢٨ هـ ٩٣٩ م ) ( وهذا الناشيء أبو العباس قد نقد عليكم
Islam is considered in its writings that discuss Muslims — as if it were a theoretical philosophical creed. Secondly — the lack of access to the books of the Mu'tazila for researchers so that a definitive judgment on their method of inquiry can be made. And everything that has reached us are brief fragments mostly about their doctrines, but their methods of inquiry have never been mentioned at all. Thirdly — the existence of books of later theologians — i.e., those who lived after the fifth century AH — led to the claim that the books of the earlier theologians — i.e., those who lived before this century — were like them. These later books are composed mostly according to the method of Aristotelian logic. And these books have reached us, while the books of the earlier theologians are either lost or not yet published. And this led to the claim that the science of kalam was influenced in all its ages by Aristotle's logic. In reality, Aristotelian logic was met in the first theological school until the fifth century with the worst reception. All theological factions attacked it: Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, Shi'a, and Karramiyya. Ibn Taymiyya says: 'The Muslim thinkers never turned to their way; rather the Ash'ariyya, Mu'tazila, Karramiyya, Shi'a, and all other groups of rational inquiry used to condemn its corruption. The first to mix their logic with the principles of Muslims was Abu Hamid al-Ghazali.' And he says in another passage: 'The Muslim thinkers after it was translated and they knew it continued to condemn and blame it and do not turn to it nor to its people in their intellectual and religious balances.' And this is echoed by Ibn al-Qayyim as well. And what proves this clearly: First — the difference in view of the theologians and the Peripatetics regarding the topic of definition (hadd) and the criticism of the former of the Aristotelian definition. Second — the theologians' rejection of the topic of Aristotelian syllogism. Ibn Khaldun (785 AH / 1406 CE) tells us: 'The Muslims did not adopt the syllogisms because of their association with philosophical sciences that are contrary to the creed.' And excerpts have reached us from the book 'al-Ara' wa al-Diyanat' by Ibn al-Nawbakhti al-Shi'i, which includes criticism of the first figure. Third — the analytical criticism of the texts of many theologians has shown us that al-Baqillani (1 Safar 403 AH) and Imam al-Haramayn, and many of the Mu'tazila departed in their investigations from Aristotle's logic. And Abu Sulayman al-Sijistani explicitly stated this criticism, and this will come later. Fourth — the many books composed criticizing Aristotelian logic, such as the book 'al-Daqaiq' by Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib and 'al-Ara' wa al-Diyanat' by Ibn al-Nawbakhti, and many texts indicate that Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, and al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar wrote criticizing Aristotelian logic. And Abu al-Abbas al-Nashi' al-Mu'tazili criticized Aristotelian logic as mentioned in the debate of al-Sirafi (368 AH / 978 CE) with Abu Bishr Matta ibn Yunus (328 AH / 939 CE) (and this al-Nashi' Abu al-Abbas indeed criticized you).
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a historical-critical argument demonstrating that early Muslim theologians (mutakallimun) rejected Aristotelian logic, contrary to the assumption that Islamic theology was always influenced by Greek logic. The author begins by identifying three reasons for the mistaken belief that theology was tied to Aristotle: first, a flawed characterization of Islam as a philosophical creed; second, the unavailability of early Mu'tazilite works; third, the survival of later theological works that adopted Aristotelian form, leading to anachronistic assumptions about earlier works. Then the author asserts a historical fact: until the fifth century AH, Aristotelian logic was vehemently opposed by all theological schools. Ibn Taymiyya and Ibn al-Qayyim are quoted to confirm this, with Ibn Taymiyya explicitly stating that al-Ghazali was the first to mix logic with Islamic principles. To substantiate this claim, the author lists four pieces of evidence: (1) the different approach of theologians to definition (hadd) and their critique of the Aristotelian definition; (2) the rejection of Aristotelian syllogism, with Ibn Khaldun's testimony and Ibn al-Nawbakhti's criticism of the first figure; (3) analytical studies showing that early theologians like al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn departed from Aristotle; (4) the existence of numerous works criticizing Aristotelian logic, citing specific authors and a famous debate. Each piece supports the overarching thesis that early Islamic theology was independent of Greek logical methodology.

Ibn Hazm's Islamic Critique of Aristotelian Logic
Sa'id al-Andalusi reports Ibn Hazm's logical work and his criticism of Aristotle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key features of Ibn Hazm's critique and evaluate Sa'id al-Andalusi's historical judgment.

Core Idea
Ibn Hazm replaced Greek examples with Islamic ones, but his critique was judged flawed for not mastering Aristotle.

This passage from Sa'id al-Andalusi gives us a window into the early Islamic reception of Aristotelian logic. Ibn Hazm, the famous Zahiri jurist, attempted to reform logic using Islamic sources, but his contemporary Sa'id criticizes him for misunderstanding Aristotle. Let's dissect the critique and the counter-critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Hazm's innovation
Find what Ibn Hazm substituted in his logic book. The passage mentions 'amthila fiqhiyya' (jurisprudential examples) and 'jawami' shar'iyya' (Shari'ic universals).
These are explicitly contrasted with Greek examples.
2
Identify Sa'id's specific criticism
Look for the phrase 'mukhalafa min lam yafham gharadahu' – contradiction of one who did not understand. Sa'id faults Ibn Hazm for not understanding Aristotle's purpose nor being well-versed in his works.
This is a methodological critique, not a doctrinal one.
3
Evaluate Sa'id's judgment
Consider whether Sa'id's criticism is itself valid. Did Ibn Hazm actually misunderstand Aristotle, or did he intentionally diverge for Islamically grounded reasons? Also note the historical context: later scholars like Ibn Taymiyya also critiqued Aristotle.
This evaluation helps assess the reliability of the report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر عن ابن حزم أنه نقد منطق أرسطو يقول صاعد « وألف كتاباً أسماه التقريب لحدود المنطق ، بسط فيه القول على تبيين المعارف واستعمل فيه أمثلة فقهية وجوامع شرعية وخالف أرسططاليس واضع هذا العلم في بعض أصوله مخالفة من لم يفهم غرضه ولا ارتاض في كتبه ، فكتابه من أجل هذا كثير الغلط بين السقط »
And he mentions about Ibn Hazm that he criticized Aristotle's logic. Sa'id says: 'And he authored a book named 'al-Taqrib li-Hudud al-Mantiq', in which he expanded speech on clarifying cognitions, and used in it jurisprudential examples and Shari'ic universals, and he contradicted Aristotle, the founder of this science, in some of its principles, in the manner of one who did not understand his purpose nor exercised in his books; thus his book for this reason is full of error and weakness.'
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a historical report from the scholar Sa'id al-Andalusi (d. 1070 CE) about Ibn Hazm's (d. 1064 CE) work on logic. It describes Ibn Hazm's book 'al-Taqrib li-Hudud al-Mantiq' which aimed to explain knowledge using examples from fiqh and Shari'ah. The passage notes that Ibn Hazm contradicted Aristotle on some fundamental points, but Sa'id criticizes Ibn Hazm for not properly understanding Aristotle's aims nor being thoroughly acquainted with his works. Therefore, Sa'id judges the book as containing many errors and lapses. This illustrates early Islamic critiques of Aristotelian logic by scholars who preferred an Islamically grounded methodology.

أمثلة فقهية
جوامع شرعية

The Cause Must Be Effective
Author enumerates the first condition with comparative remarks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain what it means for a legal cause to be effective in usul al-fiqh and distinguish it from Mill's conception of cause as invariable antecedent.

Core Idea
Effectiveness means the ruling exists because of the cause, not merely after it.

The first condition for a valid cause in analogical reasoning is that it be mu'aththira (effective). This lesson unpacks al-Baqillani's definition and contrasts it with John Stuart Mill's view, showing how Islamic legal theory grounds causation in intrinsic connection rather than mere regularity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being discussed
Locate the first condition in the Arabic text: 'أن تكون العلة مؤثرة في الحكم'. Note that it is the first of three conditions.
The term mu'aththira literally means 'effective' or 'producing an effect'.
2
Understand al-Baqillani's definition
Read al-Baqillani's explanation: the ruling occurs upon the cause's existence because of it, not because of anything else. This ties the ruling directly to the cause as the sole reason.
This is a epistemological condition: it is about the mujtahid's dominant conjecture (ghalabat al-zann).
3
Contrast with Mill's view
The author notes that for Mill, a cause is merely an invariable unconditional antecedent. In Islamic law, effectiveness goes beyond regularity—the cause is not just what comes before, but what necessitates.
Mill's causation is mechanical; Islamic legal causation is teleological.
4
Consider Bacon's influence
The author remarks that Muslim jurists are closer to Bacon, who saw cause as the very substance of the thing (ipsissima res). Example: intoxication is essential to the prohibition of khamr, not just a concomitant.
This shows the cause is intrinsic, not extrinsic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شروط العلة :
اشترط الأصوليون للعلة شروطاً ، متعددة ، غير أنهم لا يتفقون على تلك الشروط جميعها ، بل يختلفون في بعضها . ولن يعنينا نحن هذا النزاع بقدر ما تعنينا توضيح العناصر المنطقية في الشروط .
أولاً ـ أن تكون العلة مؤثرة في الحكم لأن الحكم معلول لها ، فان لم يكن لها ثمة تأثير فيه خرجت عن كونها علة(١) . ويفسر كون العلة مؤثرة في الحكم قول الباقلاني « هو أن يغلب على ظن المجتهد أن الحكم حاصل عند ثبوتها لأجلها دون شيء سواها »(٢) هنا يختلف المسلمون عن مل . فالعلة عند مل لا تكون مؤثرة « إنما هي المقدم غير المختلف وغير المشروط ـ غير المشروط بمعنى أنه يكفي في إحداث المعلول أي فرض في أي ظروف فرضت » . وإذا كان الأصوليون يبتعدون عن مل في تعريف العلة ، فإنهم كانوا أقرب إلى مذهب بيكون ، فالعلة عند بيكون ليست مقدماً فحسب ولكن هي مقوم الشيء نفسه ipsissma rds(٣) . ( الإسكار ذاتي أو لازم ) .
ثانياً ـ أن تكون العلة وصفاً منضبطاً غير مضطرب(٤) « أي أن يكون تأثيرها لحكمة مقصودة لا لحكمة مجردة لخفائها(٥) ـ ويوجب هذا أن تكون « ظاهرة جلية »(٦) وإلا فلا يمكن نقلها إلى الفرع ، وخاصة إذا ما كانت أخفى من الفرع أو مساوية له في الخفاء . وأن تكون « سالمة أي لا يردها نص أو إجماع » وألا تكون « معترضة » بعلل أقوى منها وأن تكون أوصافها « مسلمة » أو مدلولاً عليها . وألا توجب للفرع حكماً وللأصل حكماً آخر غيره ـ وألا توجب ضدين لأنها تكون حينئذ منتجة لحكمين متضادين(٧) . ولا نجد لهذه الشروط شبيهاً في المنطق الحديث .
ثالثاً ـ أن تكون العلة مطردة « أي كلما وجدت العلة في صورة من الصور وجد الحكم » أي تدور العلة مع الحكم وجوداً ـ فكلما ظهرت ظهر ـ ومثال
Conditions of the cause:
The legal theorists stipulated multiple conditions for the cause, but they do not agree on all of those conditions; rather, they differ regarding some of them. This dispute does not concern us as much as clarifying the logical elements in the conditions.
First – that the cause be effective in the ruling, because the ruling is an effect of it. So if it has no effect in it, it ceases to be a cause(1). Al-Baqillani explains the cause being effective in the ruling by saying: “It is that the jurist’s dominant conjecture is that the ruling occurs upon its existence because of it, not because of anything else”(2). Here the Muslims differ from Mill. For Mill, the cause is not effective; rather, “it is merely the antecedent that is invariable and unconditional – unconditional meaning that it suffices to produce the effect under any circumstances.” And if the legal theorists depart from Mill in defining the cause, they were closer to Bacon’s doctrine. For Bacon, the cause is not merely an antecedent but is the very substance of the thing itself, ipsissma res(3). (Intoxication is essential or necessary.)
Second – that the cause be a precise, non-fluctuating attribute(4), “i.e., that its effect be for an intended wisdom, not for a bare wisdom due to its hiddenness”(5). This requires it to be “manifest and clear”(6); otherwise it cannot be transferred to the branch, especially if it is more hidden than the branch or equal to it in hiddenness. It must be “sound, i.e., not contradicted by a text or consensus,” not “opposed” by stronger causes, its attributes must be “accepted” or indicated. It must not entail for the branch a ruling different from that of the original, and must not entail two opposites, because then it would produce two contradictory rulings(7). We find no parallel to these conditions in modern logic.
Third – that the cause be consistent, “i.e., whenever the cause is found in a case, the ruling is found,” i.e., the cause co-occurs with the ruling existentially – whenever it appears, it appears – and an example...
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage enumerates three essential conditions for a valid cause ('illa) in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh), which is central to analogical reasoning (qiyas). The author first notes that jurists disagree on some conditions but focuses on logical aspects. The first condition: the cause must be effective (mu'aththira) in producing the ruling. Al-Baqillani defines effectiveness as the dominant conjecture that the ruling occurs because of the cause alone. The author contrasts this with John Stuart Mill's notion of cause as an invariable antecedent, and notes that Muslim jurists are closer to Francis Bacon's view that the cause is the substance of the thing, exemplified by intoxication being essential to the prohibition of khamr. The second condition: the cause must be a precise, non-fluctuating attribute (mundabit). This means its effect is for a manifest wisdom, so the cause must be clear and not hidden, transferable to branches, sound (not contradicted by text or consensus), not opposed by stronger causes, not producing inconsistent rulings. The author observes that modern logic has no parallel to these conditions. The third condition: the cause must be consistent (mutṭarida), meaning it always accompanies the ruling: whenever the cause is present, the ruling is present. The text cuts off before completing the example, but the condition is fully stated.

Key Terms
العلة مؤثرة
غلبان الظن
المقدم غير المختلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does al-Baqillani define an effective cause?
A) It is the invariable antecedent that always precedes the ruling.
B) The jurist's dominant conjecture that the ruling occurs because of the cause, not because of something else.
C) The cause must be hidden and known only to God.
D) The cause must be unconditional in the sense used by Mill.

Key Takeaway
An effective cause in usul al-fiqh is one that produces the ruling intrinsically, not just as a regular antecedent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the cause for the prohibition of riba (usury): excess (fadl) in a specific exchange. Write a brief explanation of how this cause satisfies the effectiveness condition according to al-Baqillani's definition.

The Cause Must Be Precise and Consistent
Author enumerates the second and third conditions with sub‑requirements and a comparative remark.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the requirements of a precise (mundabit) and consistent (mutṭarida) cause in usul al-fiqh and explain why each is necessary for sound analogy.

Core Idea
A valid cause must be clearly defined and always accompany the ruling.

The second and third conditions for a valid cause are precision (al-inḍibāṭ) and consistency (al-iṭṭirād). The cause must be a clear, non‑fluctuating attribute that is manifest and not hidden, and it must always co‑occur with the ruling. This lesson examines these two conditions and their sub‑requirements, noting that modern logic has no parallel to the precision condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second condition
Find the phrase 'ثانياً – أن تكون العلة وصفاً منضبطاً' and note the term mundabit. This means the cause must be precise and non‑fluctuating.
The word implies an attribute that is stable and clearly defined.
2
Explore the sub‑conditions of precision
The author lists several sub‑requirements: the cause must be manifest (ẓāhira jaliyya), sound (salīma), not opposed by stronger causes, its attributes accepted or indicated, and it must not produce contradictory rulings.
These ensure the cause can be reliably transferred to new cases.
3
Understand the third condition
Read 'ثالثاً – أن تكون العلة مطردة' and the gloss: whenever the cause is found, the ruling is found. This is co‑extensiveness in existence.
The passage cuts off but the condition is fully stated.
4
Contrast with modern logic
The author remarks that there is no parallel in modern logic for these detailed conditions, especially the precision requirement. This highlights the unique rigor of Islamic legal reasoning.
Modern logic typically does not impose such epistemological and practical constraints on causal terms.

Sabr wa Taqsim: Stoic, Not Aristotelian Origin
Author argues origin of sabr wa taqsim from Stoics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Stoic and Aristotelian origins of sabr wa taqsim and evaluate the author's claim about Muslim independence from Greek logic.

Core Idea
Sabr wa taqsim is Stoic, not Aristotelian, and Muslims did not borrow it from Greek logic.

This lesson explores the historical origin of the method known as sabr wa taqsim (division and classification), which is used in usul al-fiqh to identify the cause ('illa) of a ruling. The author makes two key claims: the idea came from Stoic logic, not Aristotelian, and Muslims did not derive it from Greek logic at all. Understanding this historical backdrop helps us assess the method's epistemological status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main historical claim about sabr wa taqsim
Notice that the author explicitly states that the idea of sabr wa taqsim came from Greek logic, but specifically from the Stoics, not from Aristotle. The author also asserts with certainty that Muslims did not take it from Greek logic.
The author uses 'أستطيع أن أجزم' (I can assert) indicating strong conviction.
2
Trace the reasoning for the Stoic origin
The author grounds the Stoic attribution on the fact that Aristotle did not know conditional syllogisms, whether conjunctive (muttasil) or disjunctive (munfasil). This implies that the method of division and classification, which involves conditional reasoning, must have come from later Stoic logic.
This is a historical argument based on the scope of Aristotelian logic.
3
Evaluate the claim about Muslim independence
Consider whether the author provides evidence for the claim that Muslims did not take sabr wa taqsim from Greek logic. The passage does not give a source or argument here; it is a assertion. Reflect on what other evidence might support or challenge this.
This claim is controversial; many later Muslim logicians integrated Greek ideas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكرة السبر والتقسيم من منطق اليونان . فينبغي أن نلاحظ أنهم لم يستمدوها من منطق أرسطو بل من منطق الرواقيين فأرسطو لم يعرف ـ كما بينا من قبل الأقيسة الشرطية متصلة كانت أو منفصلة . غير أني أستطيع أن أجزم بأن المسلمين لم يأخذوا فكرة السبر والتقسيم عن المنطق اليوناني . وسنرى كثيرين من المناطقة المتأخرين يعتبرون السبر والتقسيم ـ إذا ما اعتبرناه طريقاً قائماً بذاته لا مسلكاً للعلة ـ ضعيفاً ولا يفيد اليقين . وهذا ما يجعله مخالفاً لطبيعة القياس حملياً كان أو شرطياً ـ فإن هذا القياس يؤدي إلى اليقين .
The idea of sabr wa taqsim (division and classification) from Greek logic. It should be noted that they did not derive it from Aristotelian logic but from Stoic logic, for Aristotle did not know — as we have shown before — conditional syllogisms, whether conjunctive or disjunctive. However, I can assert that Muslims did not take the idea of sabr wa taqsim from Greek logic. We will see many later logicians consider sabr wa taqsim — if we consider it an independent method rather than a path to the cause — to be weak and not yielding certainty. And this makes it contrary to the nature of the syllogism, whether categorical or conditional, for this syllogism leads to certainty.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a critical historical remark about the origin of the method known as sabr wa taqsim (division and classification), which is used in jurisprudential reasoning to identify the 'illa (cause) of a ruling. The author first notes that the idea comes from Greek logic, but specifically from Stoic logic, not Aristotelian, since Aristotle was unaware of conditional syllogisms. The author then asserts with certainty that Muslims did not borrow this method from Greek logic at all. The passage further critiques the method itself by referencing later Muslim logicians who consider sabr wa taqsim, when taken as an independent method rather than a sub-method for finding the cause, to be weak and incapable of producing certainty. This is contrasted with the syllogism (qiyas), whether categorical or conditional, which does yield certainty. Thus, the author is positioning sabr wa taqsim as inferior to syllogistic reasoning in terms of epistemic certainty.

Key Terms
السبر والتقسيم
المنطق اليوناني
المنطق الأرسطي
المنطق الرواقي
القياس الشرطي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the Stoics were more advanced in logic
B) Because Aristotle did not know conditional syllogisms
C) Because Muslims preferred Stoic logic over Aristotelian
D) Because sabr wa taqsim was used only by Stoics

Key Takeaway
Sabr wa taqsim originated from Stoic logic, not Aristotelian, and its epistemic status is weaker than syllogisms.
