Tracing the Sources of Ibn Taymiyya's Negative Critique of Logic
Author distinguishes two aspects and traces sources of negative aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sources of Ibn Taymiyya's negative critique of logic and distinguish it from his positive contributions based on the author's analysis.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya's negative critique of logic was largely borrowed from Greek skeptics, not original.

This lesson explores a key meta-analysis of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of logic. The author of Manahij Bahth argues that we must separate two aspects: a negative one (destructive) and a positive one (constructive). While the positive aspect contains original elements, the negative aspect is heavily indebted to Greek skeptics and sophists. Understanding this distinction is crucial for properly evaluating Ibn Taymiyya's place in the history of Islamic logic criticism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of Ibn Taymiyya's critique
Find in the passage the terms 'maqam salib' (negative aspect) and 'maqam mujib' (positive aspect). Notice that the author gives two reasons for separating them: independent arguments and originality difference.
2
Trace the sources of the negative aspect
According to the author, Ibn Taymiyya derived his eleven negative arguments from Greek skeptics (shukak) and sophists (sufistaiyun). The author claims no creativity in this aspect.
The author mentions that sometimes Ibn Taymiyya follows the skeptics in essence, sometimes in expression.
3
Examine the core ideas borrowed from the skeptics
The passage identifies three key issues: dawr (circularity), tasalsul (infinite regress), and relativism of everything. These were originally sophist themes later systematized by skeptics.
These issues form the backbone of the skeptical critique of Aristotelian logic.
4
Assess the author's evidence for borrowing
The author states that he has indicated the presence of these arguments in European sources about skeptics. Pay attention to the footnote marker (١) indicating external references. Consider whether the author provides sufficient comparative evidence within this passage.
The passage prepares for a list of eleven arguments that follow, which are meant to illustrate the borrowing.
5
Determine the implication for Ibn Taymiyya's originality
Based on this passage, if the negative aspect is entirely derivative, then Ibn Taymiyya's originality lies only in the positive aspect. This reframes how we evaluate his contribution to the critique of logic.
This analysis does not diminish the positive aspect but clarifies the historical genealogy of his ideas.

المقامين عن الآخر لسببين : أولهما ـ أن ابن تيمية ينقد كل مقام من هذين المقامين بحجج مستقلة . ثانيهما أن المقام السالب ليس عملاً مبتكراً . بل كان ابن تيمية مجرد ناقل فيه ، بينما دخلت عناصر مبتكرة في المقام الموجب . أما المقام السالب فوجه ابن تيمية نحو نقده إحدى عشرة حجة استمدها من الشكاك اليونانيين من ناحية ومن السوفسطائيين من ناحية . وليس فيها ـ فيما يرجح ـ أي إبداع . بل يكاد يكون فيها متابعة للشكاك في جوهر الفكر أحياناً وفي عبارتهم أحياناً أخرى . أما متابعة ابن تيمية للشكاك في جوهر فكرة نقدهم للمنطق الأرسططاليسي فهو أنه يقيم هذا النقد ـ كما أقامه الشكاك من قبل ـ على مسائل الدور أو التسلسل أو نسبية كل شيء . فتلك مسائل وضعها السوفسطائيون ، وتعارفوا عليها ثم صاغها الشكاك في مذهب منهجي . أما متابعة ابن تيمية للشكاك في عبارتهم وأمثلهم فيدو في بعض الحجج . وقد أشرنا إلى وجود هذه الحجج في المصادر الأوروبية التي وصلتنا عن الشكاك(١) وحجج ابن تيمية التي وجهها نحو نقد هذا المقام السالب هي في إيجاز :
The two aspects are distinguished from each other for two reasons: First, that Ibn Taymiyya critiques each of these two aspects with independent arguments. Second, that the negative aspect is not an innovative work. Rather, Ibn Taymiyya was merely a transmitter in it, while innovative elements entered into the positive aspect. As for the negative aspect, Ibn Taymiyya directed towards its criticism eleven arguments that he derived from Greek skeptics on one hand and from sophists on the other. In them — in what is likely — there is no creativity. Rather, he is almost following the skeptics in the essence of thought sometimes and in their expression at other times. As for Ibn Taymiyya's following of the skeptics in the essence of their idea of criticizing Aristotelian logic, it is that he based this critique — just as the skeptics did before — on the issues of circularity (dawr), infinite regress (tasalsul), or relativism of everything. These are issues that the sophists established, and they became familiar with them, then the skeptics formulated them into a systematic doctrine. As for Ibn Taymiyya's following of the skeptics in their expression and examples, it is evident in some arguments. Commentary
This passage is a meta-commentary from the author of Manahij Bahth analyzing the structure of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of logic. The author begins by stating two reasons for separating the positive and negative aspects of Ibn Taymiyya's critique: first, that Ibn Taymiyya uses independent arguments for each, and second, that the negative aspect is derivative while the positive aspect contains original elements. The focus is on the negative aspect, which the author claims is entirely borrowed from Greek skeptics and sophists. The author identifies the core ideas of the skeptics' critique — circularity, infinite regress, and relativism — and asserts that Ibn Taymiyya merely repeats them, sometimes in thought and sometimes in expression. The passage sets the stage for presenting the eleven arguments, which are listed in subsequent pages. The author's tone is evaluative, judging Ibn Taymiyya's contribution as unoriginal in the negative aspect.

Distinguishing Islamic from Greek Skepticism
Author argues Ibn Taymiyya's arguments are borrowed and distinguishes Islamic skepticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the author's critique of Ibn Taymiyya's arguments with the Islamic attitude toward Aristotelian logic, and distinguish between absolute skepticism and instrumental skepticism.

Core Idea
Greek doubt was absolute; Islamic doubt was a tool to defend its own science.

In this passage, the author directly challenges the originality of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic. He claims that the eleven arguments are essentially borrowed from the Greek sophists and skeptics. But more importantly, he draws a sharp contrast between the Greek spirit of absolute skepticism and the Islamic approach, which used skepticism selectively only to clear the ground for its own methodology. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating later critiques of logic in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's central claim
Find the sentence where the author states that Ibn Taymiyya's arguments are not new. What does he say they are?
Look for the phrase 'لا نجد... شيئاً جديداً'.
2
Trace the origin of the arguments
According to the author, where did these arguments originate? Identify the two groups mentioned.
The author mentions 'السوفسطائيين والشكاك'.
3
Distinguish the two types of skepticism
The author contrasts 'الشك المطلق' (absolute skepticism) with how Muslims used skepticism. Describe each in your own words.
Absolute skepticism doubts everything; Islamic skepticism was a means to an end.
4
Explain why Muslims rejected Greek logic
According to the author, what was the positive reason for Muslims rejecting Aristotelian logic, as opposed to pure doubt?
They had 'علماً خاصاً بهم' and methods different from the Greeks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إننا لا نجد ـ فيما يرجح ـ شيئاً جديداً في هذه الحجج الإحدى عشرة يكد يصطنع ابن تيمية حجج السوفسطائيين والشكاك . تلك الحجج التي نفذت إلى العالم الإسلامي ، كما بينا من قبل . ولم يكن هذا الشك اليوناني يعبر عن روح الحضارة الإسلامية ذاتها تجاه منطق أرسطو وفلسفته . فقد وقف الشكاك من أرسطو ومن الرواقيين ، بل ومن العلم كله ، موقف الشك المطلق أما المسلمون فلم يقفوا هذا الموقف على الإطلاق . إنهم رفضوا منطق أرسطو وعلم اليونان ، لأنهم اعتقدوا أن لهم علماً خاصاً بهم ، وطرقاً في بحث هذا العلم تخالف طرق اليونانيين . فكان الشك لديهم وسيلة فقط لهدم العلم
We do not find — as is likely — anything new in these eleven arguments; Ibn Taymiyya almost adopts the arguments of the sophists and skeptics. Those arguments which penetrated the Islamic world, as we mentioned before. And this Greek skepticism did not express the spirit of Islamic civilization itself towards Aristotle's logic and philosophy. For the skeptics took from Aristotle and the Stoics, and indeed from all knowledge, the position of absolute skepticism. But the Muslims did not take this position at all. They rejected Aristotelian logic and Greek science because they believed they had their own science, and methods in researching this science that differed from the methods of the Greeks. So skepticism for them was only a means to demolish Greek science.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is the author's critical evaluation of Ibn Taymiyya's arguments. The author asserts that the eleven arguments are not original; they are borrowed from the sophists and skeptics of Greek tradition. The author notes that this skeptical approach had already entered the Islamic world. However, the author distinguishes between the Greek skeptical attitude, which doubted all knowledge absolutely, and the Muslim attitude. Muslims rejected Aristotelian logic not out of universal doubt but because they had their own distinct science and methodology. For Muslims, skepticism was a tool to dismantle Greek science, not to deny all knowledge. This passage clarifies that Ibn Taymiyya's arguments do not represent the mainstream Islamic intellectual response to Greek logic.

Key Terms
السوفسطائيين
الشكاك
الشك المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the novelty of Ibn Taymiyya's arguments by tracing them to Greek sophists and skeptics, and emphasizes that Islamic skepticism was instrumental, not absolute.

Critiquing Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Originality and Influence
Author argues Ibn Taymiyya's eleven arguments are borrowed from Greek skeptics and not original or authentically Islamic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ways in which Ibn Taymiyya's arguments against definition are derivative of Greek skepticism, and distinguish the Islamic methodological critique from Greek philosophical skepticism.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya's arguments are not original; they mirror Greek skeptics but for opposite ends.

The author argues that Ibn Taymiyya's eleven arguments against definition are not innovative but borrowed from Greek Sophists and Skeptics. However, he emphasizes that the Islamic rejection of Aristotelian logic was motivated by a belief in an autonomous Islamic scientific method, not by skepticism as a goal. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the authenticity and strength of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
Determine what exactly the author is critiquing in Ibn Taymiyya's arguments. The passage states he adopts arguments from the Sophists and Skeptics.
2
Analyze the charge of derivative
Examine the evidence the author presents that Ibn Taymiyya's arguments are borrowed from Greek sources, not original Islamic thinking.
3
Distinguish the motivations
Note how the author differentiates Greek skepticism (skepticism as a goal) from Islamic rejection of Aristotelian logic (rejection to affirm their own science).
4
Examine the internal tension
Observe that the author acknowledges Ibn Taymiyya's own awareness of this distinction in his refutation of the positive position versus the negative position.
5
Formulate an assessment
Based on the author's critique, evaluate whether his meta-critique of Ibn Taymiyya is valid and what it reveals about the Islamic logical tradition.

إننا لا نجد ـ فيما يرجح ـ شيئاً جديداً في هذه الحجج الإحدى عشرة يكاد يصطنع ابن تيمية حجج السوفسطائيين والشكاك . تلك الحجج التي نفذت إلى العالم الإسلامي ، كما بينا من قبل . ولم يكن هذا الشك اليوناني يعبر عن روح الحضارة الإسلامية ذاتها تجاه منطق أرسطو وفلسفته . فقد وقف الشكاك من أرسطو ومن الرواقيين ، بل ومن العلم كله ، موقف الشك المطلق أما المسلمون فلم يقفوا هذا الموقف على الإطلاق . إنهم رفضوا منطق أرسطو وعلم اليونان ، لأنهم اعتقدوا أن لهم علماً خاصاً بهم ، وطرقاً في بحث هذا العلم تخالف طرق اليونانيين . فكان الشك لديهم وسيلة فقط لهدم العلم الأرسططاليسي . بينما الشك لدى الشكاك اليونانيين ، غاية الحكيم الحق . فهناك فارق كبير بين الروحين . ولذلك تبدو حجج ابن تيمية التي ذكرناها غريبة عن الروح الإسلامية . يكاد ابن تيمية يدرك - إلى حد ما - هذه الحقيقة في نقضه للمقام الموجب « الحد يفيد تصور الأشياء » حقاً إنه يتخذ في نقضه بعض حجج من الشكاك ، غير أن موقفه يختلف في نقض هذا في المقام الموجب عن موقفه في نقض المقام السالب . وذلك في محاولته أن يربط بين نقده وبين النزعة الإسلامية في نقدها للمنطق الأرسططاليسي ، كما سنوضح هذا في مكانه - علاوة على تخلل عناصر في نقده ، أرجح أنها رواقية .
We do not find – in all likelihood – anything new in these eleven arguments. Ibn Taymiyya almost adopts the arguments of the Sophists and Skeptics – those arguments that infiltrated the Islamic world, as we have shown before. This Greek skepticism did not express the spirit of Islamic civilization itself towards Aristotle's logic and philosophy. The Skeptics took a position of absolute skepticism towards Aristotle, the Stoics, and indeed all science. But Muslims never took this position at all. They rejected Aristotle's logic and Greek science because they believed they had their own science and methods of researching it that differed from the methods of the Greeks. So for them, skepticism was only a means to destroy Aristotelian science. Whereas for the Greek Skeptics, skepticism was the true sage's goal. There is a great difference between the two spirits. Therefore, the arguments of Ibn Taymiyya that we have mentioned appear alien to the Islamic spirit. Ibn Taymiyya almost realizes – to some extent – this truth in his refutation of the positive position 'Definition conveys knowledge of things'. Indeed, in his refutation he adopts some arguments from the Skeptics. However, his position in refuting this positive position differs from his position in refuting the negative position. This is in his attempt to link his critique with the Islamic tendency in its critique of Aristotelian logic, as we will clarify in its place – besides the presence of elements in his critique that I deem most likely Stoic.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is the author's meta-critique of Ibn Taymiyya's eleven arguments against definition. The author contends that these arguments lack originality and are derivative from Greek Sophists and Skeptics. He emphasizes a fundamental difference in motivation: Greek Skeptics doubted for its own sake, considering skepticism the goal of wisdom, while Muslims who rejected Aristotelian logic did so to affirm an independent Islamic scientific method. Thus, Ibn Taymiyya's use of skeptical arguments is out of step with the Islamic intellectual tradition. The author notes that Ibn Taymiyya himself seems aware of this tension when he distinguishes between refuting the positive claim that definitions give knowledge and refuting the negative claim. In the positive critique, Ibn Taymiyya tries to connect his arguments with Islamic criticisms, but the author suspects Stoic influences. The passage sets up the next section where Ibn Taymiyya's specific arguments for the positive position are examined.

Key Terms
السوفسطائيين (al-sufasta'iyyin): Greek Sophists who relativized knowledge; used as a label for those who deny objective truth. [Note: In Islamic contexts, sufasta'iyya often refers to extreme relativists or those who reject the possibility of certain knowledge.]
الشكاك (al-shukkak): Skeptics who doubt the possibility of certain knowledge; here specifically Greek Pyrrhonian skeptics. [Note: In Islamic polemics, shakk can mean doubters who reject revelation, not necessarily philosophical skeptics.]
المقام الموجب (al-maqam al-mujib): The positive claim that definition indeed conveys knowledge of things, as defended by logicians. [Note: This term is specific to this debate; not commonly used outside Ibn Taymiyya's critique.]
المقام السالب (al-maqam al-salib): The negative claim that definition does not convey knowledge; the position of those who deny the utility of definitions. [Note: Same as above.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They were absolute skeptics like the Greeks.
B) They believed they had their own distinct science and methods.
C) They found the Greek arguments unconvincing.
D) They wanted to adopt Stoic logic instead.

Key Takeaway
The critique of Ibn Taymiyya's arguments reveals that not all rejections of Aristotelian logic are grounded in Islamic methodology; some are derivative of Greek skepticism, and the true Islamic spirit affirms an autonomous science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Sources of Ibn Taymiyya's Destructive Critique of Definition
Author identifies three sources for Ibn Taymiyya's destructive critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three sources of Ibn Taymiyya's destructive critique of definition and evaluate their relative contributions.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya's destructive critique is not original; it depends on Sophists, Stoics, and Mutakallimun.

In this passage, the author traces the intellectual genealogy of Ibn Taymiyya's destructive critique of the Aristotelian theory of definition. While Ibn Taymiyya appears innovative, his arguments often derive from earlier sources: Greek Sophists and Skeptics, Stoic nominalism, and crucially, the Islamic theologians (mutakallimun) who transmitted these ideas. Understanding these sources helps us evaluate the originality and force of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish destructive vs. constructive critique
The author partitions Ibn Taymiyya's critique into a destructive aspect (al-janib al-hadmi) and a constructive aspect. Identify which part is being analyzed here and note that the destructive part draws heavily on external sources.
2
Identify the first source: Sophists and Skeptics
The passage states that Greek Sophists and Skeptics are the ultimate source. Note that Ibn Taymiyya does not name them but likely accessed their arguments through mutakallimun or Islamic commentators.
3
Identify the second source: Stoics
The Stoics contributed a nominalist denial of extramental universals, which aligns with Ibn Taymiyya's critique of mahiyya. However, the author notes uncertainty due to lack of historical clarity.
4
Identify the third and direct source: Mutakallimun
The Islamic theologians are the most important direct source. They transmitted skeptical arguments and also developed nominalist ideas. Ibn Taymiyya quotes them and likely obtained his knowledge of the other sources from them.
5
Assess the claim of originality
The author argues that apart from some personal thinking, the destructive critique is largely derived. Consider whether this undermines Ibn Taymiyya's project or simply shows his engagement with the tradition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبقدر ما كان ابن تيمية في المقام السالب وفي الجزء الأول من المقام الموجب ناقلاً لحجج الشكاك بقدر ما حاول أن يظهر في هذا الجزء الثاني من نقد المقام الموجب آراء خاصة في نقد الحد الأرسططاليسي ، بحيث يمكننا أن نقرر بأن هذا الجزء الأخير هو العمل المبتكر لابن تيمية في نقد الحد الأرسططاليسي في جانبه الهدمي . أما بقية عناصر نقد هذا الجانب فيونانية في جوهرها وحقيقتها ، وما من حجة منها إلا ويمكن أن يرد على ابن تيمية فيها بما رد هو على المنطق الأرسططاليسي من أنها تخالف بديهة العقل ، أو أنها مجرد وضع واصطلاح ، وغير هذا مما وجهه من ردود . (١) ابن تيمية . الرد . ٦٧ - ٧٦ . السيوطي صون ص ٢١٧ . ٢ - مصادر الجانب الهدمي لنقد نظرية الحد : إن مصادر ابن تيمية في هذا الجانب هم السوفسطائيون والشكاك اليونانيون . ولا يذكر ابن تيمية أسمائهم ولا يشير إليهم على الإطلاق . ومن المؤكد أن ابن تيمية اطلع على آراء الشكاك والسوفسطائيين إما في كتب المتكلمين ، وإما في كتب الشراح الإسلاميين للفلسفة اليونانية . ويحاول ابن تيمية أحياناً أن يربط هذه الحجج بنزعته الخاصة في نقد المنطق الأرسططاليسي وأحياناً ينقلها بألفاظها وأمثلتها . فالسوفسطائيون والشكاك إذن هم المصدر النهائي لابن تيمية في نقده لمبحث الحد الأرسططاليسي . المصدر الثاني - هم الرواقيون . ومن المحتمل أن يكون ابن تيمية قد عرف بعض آرائهم ، فان نقده للماهية يشبه نقد الرواقيين لها . أي أن ابن تيمية يتفق مع الرواقية في الاتجاه الإسمي الذي ينكر وجود الكلي في الخارج وبالتالي وجود الجنس والفصل كعنصري الحد . ولكن إن الغموض الذي يحيط بتاريخ الرواقية يحول دون الحكم القاطع في أمثال هذه المسائل . المصدر الثالث - المتكلمون - وهم من أهم مصادر ابن تيمية . ينقل عنهم ويذكر عباراتهم ويشير إلى كتبهم - ويرجح كثيراً أن يكون ابن تيمية قد عرف ما عرفه عن السوفسطائيين والشكاك والرواقيين منهم . فهم - كما قلنا - يرددون حجج الشكاك من ناحية ، ومن ناحية أخرى تكلم بعضهم عن المذهب الإسمي - وخاضوا في مشكلة وجود الكلي - الكلي العقلي والكلي الطبيعي والكلي المنطقي - وهل هذه الكليات موجودة في الخارج أم غير موجودة؟ فالمتكلمون إذن هم المصدر المباشر للجانب الهدمي لنقد ابن تيمية للحد الأرسططاليسي ، استفاد من آرائهم من ناحية ، ومن ناحية أخرى نقل عنهم ما نقلوه هم عن الشكاك والرواقية ، هذه المصادر الثلاثة - مع بعض التفكير الخاص - تكون الجانب الأول من نقد ابن تيمية للحد الأرسططاليسي . وسننتقل الآن الى بحث الجانب الإنشائي لنقد ابن تيمية لقسم المنطق الأرسططاليسي الأول .
And just as Ibn Taymiyya in the negative part and in the first part of the positive aspect was a transmitter of the arguments of the skeptics, so too he tried to show in this second part of the critique of the positive aspect his own views in criticizing the Aristotelian definition, such that we can assert that this last part is Ibn Taymiyya's innovative work in criticizing the Aristotelian definition in its destructive aspect. As for the rest of the elements of the critique of this aspect, they are Greek in their essence and reality, and there is no argument among them except that it is possible to refute Ibn Taymiyya with what he himself refuted Aristotelian logic with: that they contradict the intuition of the intellect, or that they are merely arbitrary convention and terminology, and other such refutations he directed. (1) Ibn Taymiyya, al-Radd, 67-76; al-Suyuti, Sawun, p. 217. 2 - Sources of the destructive aspect of the critique of the theory of definition: Indeed, the sources of Ibn Taymiyya in this aspect are the Greek Sophists and Skeptics. Ibn Taymiyya does not mention their names nor refer to them at all. It is certain that Ibn Taymiyya became acquainted with the views of the Skeptics and Sophists either in the books of the mutakallimun or in the books of the Islamic commentators on Greek philosophy. Ibn Taymiyya sometimes tries to connect these arguments with his own tendency in criticizing Aristotelian logic, and sometimes he transmits them verbatim with their examples. So the Sophists and Skeptics are thus the ultimate source for Ibn Taymiyya in his critique of the Aristotelian topic of definition. The second source – the Stoics. It is probable that Ibn Taymiyya knew some of their views, for his critique of essence resembles the Stoic critique of it. That is, Ibn Taymiyya agrees with Stoicism in the nominalist tendency that denies the existence of the universal externally, and consequently the existence of genus and differentia as elements of definition. However, the obscurity surrounding the history of Stoicism prevents a decisive judgment on such matters. The third source – the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) – and they are among the most important sources of Ibn Taymiyya. He transmits from them, mentions their statements, and refers to their books. It is highly likely that Ibn Taymiyya learned what he learned about the Sophists, Skeptics, and Stoics from them. For they – as we said – repeat the arguments of the Skeptics on one hand, and on the other hand, some of them spoke about the nominalist school and delved into the problem of the existence of the universal – the mental universal, the natural universal, and the logical universal – and whether these universals exist externally or not. So the mutakallimun are thus the direct source for the destructive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian definition. He benefited from their views on one hand, and on the other hand he transmitted from them what they themselves had transmitted from the Skeptics and Stoics. These three sources – together with some personal thinking – constitute the first aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian definition. We will now move on to discuss the constructive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the first section of Aristotelian logic.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage provides an analysis of the sources underlying Ibn Taymiyya's destructive critique (al-janib al-hadmi) of the Aristotelian theory of definition. The author first notes that while Ibn Taymiyya was largely a transmitter of skeptics' arguments in his earlier negative and positive critiques, in this second part of the positive aspect he developed his own original views. Nevertheless, many of the arguments remain Greek in origin and could be turned against Ibn Taymiyya using his own method of refuting Aristotelian logic as contrary to intuition or as mere convention. The author then enumerates three sources for the destructive aspect. The first source is the Greek Sophists and Skeptics, whom Ibn Taymiyya does not name explicitly but whose arguments he accessed through the works of the mutakallimun or Islamic commentators on Greek philosophy. Sometimes Ibn Taymiyya adapts these arguments to his own approach, sometimes he simply repeats them verbatim. The second source is the Stoics, particularly their nominalist tendency (al-madhhab al-ismi) which denies extramental existence of universals, and thus denies that genus and species are real components of definition. However, the lack of clear historical records on Stoicism makes this uncertain. The third and most important direct source is the Islamic theologians (mutakallimun). Ibn Taymiyya quotes them extensively and likely obtained his knowledge of the Sophists, Skeptics, and Stoics from them. The mutakallimun themselves transmitted the arguments of the Skeptics and also discussed nominalist views and the problem of the reality of universals (mental, natural, and logical). Thus, the combination of these three sources, along with Ibn Taymiyya's own original thinking, forms the destructive part of his critique. The passage concludes by indicating that the discussion will now move to the constructive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.

Key Terms
السوفسطائيون
الشكاك
الرواقيون
المتكلمون
المذهب الإسمي

Ibn Taymiyya's Contradiction on Causality
Critique of Ibn Taymiyya's internal contradiction on causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradiction in Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Ash'ari occasionalism and evaluate his account of experience.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya attacks Ash'ari habit but unknowingly returns to it through repeated conjunction.

Ibn Taymiyya famously critiques Ash'ari occasionalism, accusing them of reducing causality to mere habit. Yet when he explains how empirical knowledge (tajriba) works, he grounds it in the repetition of conjunction—the very same principle he attacked. This lesson dissects this contradiction and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the attack
Find where Ibn Taymiyya accuses the Ash'aris of denying real causality and instead attributing constant conjunction to God's mere will. Note his polemical label 'Jahm'.
2
Extract the affirmative position
Locate his statements affirming Aristotelian causality: fire has a power to heat, water to cool, etc. He asserts real quiddities and substances in causes.
3
Analyze the account of tajriba
Read his definition and examples of experience: he says it is knowledge from repeated conjunction, either through one's own senses or others' testimony.
He even says the cause of empirical knowledge is repetition of association.
4
Compare and conclude
Notice that his foundation for tajriba—repeated conjunction—is identical to the Ash'ari concept of 'ada (habit). Recognize the contradiction: he attacks the Ash'aris for something he himself adopts.

ولكن ابن تيمية ما يلبث ان يسقط سقطة عظمى ، فيهاجم الأشاعرة وينسبهم إلى الجهم . ولا يفهم موقف الأشاعرة ، وإن كان قد أخذ بفكرتهم التي أضافوها إلى العلم الإنساني وهي الدوران والمناسبة والسبر والتقسيم ، فيقول لكن من لا يثبت الأسباب والعلل من أهل الكلام كالجهم وموافقيه في ذلك مثل أبي الحسن ( أي الأشعري وأتباعه ) يجعل المعلوم اقتران أحد الأمرين بالآخر لمحض مشيئة القادر المريد من غير أن يكون أحدهما مسبباً للآخر ولا مولداً له » لم يفهم ابن تيمية أبداً موقف الأشاعرة في فكرة الاقتران ، بينما فكرة العادة ، وفكرة الدوران والمناسبة والسبر والتقسيم إنما هي منبثقة من الاقتران ونقد العلية ، ويقوم عليها نقد المنطق الأرسططاليسي في نظريته الهامة عن العلية . لقد أعمت عداوة ابن تيمية لأهل السنة والجماعة بصيرته وأغلقت فكرة ، فأثبت الأسباب ، وذكر « كما يعلم اقتران أحدهما بالآخر ، فنعلم أن في النار قوة تقتضي التسخين ، وفي الماء قوة تقتضي التبريد وكذلك في العين قوة تقتضي الأبصار وفي اللسان قوة تقتضي الذوق » ، أو بمعنى موجز إنه أثبت العلية الأرسططاليسية ، فأثبت بقوله هو ـ الغريزة أو النحيزة ، أو الخلق(۱) أو بمعنى أدق أثبت في السبب « ماهية » وجوهراً » هو مسبب المسبب ، وبهذا خرج ابن تيمية هنا عن روح المذهب الإسلامي ، واعتنق أكبر الأفكار التي يقوم عليها المنطق الأرسططاليسي . غير أنه يعود إلى تفسير للتجربة والأساس التي تقوم عليه يقربه إلى حد من نظرية الأشاعرة . فيرى أن لفظ التجربة يستخدم « فيما جربه الإنسان بعقله وحسه ، وإن لم يكن من مقدوراته» ومن الأمثلة على هذا : أنه إذا طلعت الشمس ، انتشر الضوء ، وإذا غابت أظلم الجو ، وإذا بعدت الشمس عن الرؤوس ، جاء البرد ، وإذا جاء البرد ، سقط ورق الشجر ، وبرد ظاهر الأرض وسخن باطنها ، وإذا قربت من سمت الرؤوس ، جاء الحر ، وإذا جاء الحر ، أورقت الأشجار وأزهرت . ويقرر ابن تيمية ان هذا أمر يشترك فيه الناس جميعاً ، فانهم قد اعتادوه وجربوه . ولكن على أساس يقوم هذا يرى ابن تيمية أن « من يثبت الأسباب » يقيم فكرته على أساس أن « شبيه الشيء ينجذب إليه ، وضده هارب به » ويعطي ابن تيمية أمثلة تجريبية تثبت هذا . إذا برد الهواء برد ظاهر الأرض وبرد ما على ظاهر الأرض ، فتهرب السخونة إلى البواطن ، فيسخن جوف الأرض ، ويسخن ما في جوفه من ماء . ولهذا تكون الينابيع حارة في الشتاء ، وتكون أجواف الحيوان حارة ، فتأكل في الشتاء أكثر مما تأكل في الصيف ، بسبب هضم الحرارة للطعام . وإذا كان الصيف ، سخن الهواء ، فتسخن الظواهر ، وتهرب البرودة إلى البواطن . فيبرد باطن الأرض وأجواف الحيوان ، وتبرد الينابيع . ولهذا يكون الماء النابع في الصيف أبرد منه في الشتاء ، وتكون أجواف الحيوان باردة ، فتأكل في الصيف أقل منها في الشتاء ، بسبب ضعف الهضم للطعام . هذه القضايا هي المجربات العاديات ـ عند ابن تيمية ، ويرى ابن تيمية أن التجربة من فعل الإنسان ومن فعل غيره . يقول : « وقد يعلم الإنسان من فعل غيره ما يحصل له العلم « التجربي » وإن لم يكن له قدرة على فعل الغير « فالتجربة ليست فردية ، بل قد تكون تجربة الغير . كذلك يقرر أيضاً أن من أسباب التجربة أو « السبب المقتضي للعلم بالمجربات هو تكرر اقتران أحد الأمرين بالآخر ، إما مطلقاً وإما بالشعور بالمناسب . وهنا أيضاً يطبق المبدأ السابق من أن التجربة بفعل الإنسان وبفعل غيره ـ فتكرر الإقتران أيضاً
But Ibn Taymiyya soon falls into a great mistake: he attacks the Ash'aris and accuses them of Jahmism. He does not understand the Ash'ari position, although he took their idea that they added to human knowledge—namely, correlation, suitability, and trial and division. He says: 'But those who do not affirm causes and effects from among the theologians, like Jahm and his followers, such as Abu al-Hasan (i.e., al-Ash'ari and his followers), make the known association of one thing with another due to mere will of the Powerful and Willing, without one being a cause of the other or a generator of it.' Ibn Taymiyya never understood the Ash'ari position regarding the idea of association (iqtiran), while the idea of habit ('ada), and the idea of correlation, suitability, and trial and division, are derived from association and the critique of causality, and upon them is built the critique of Aristotelian logic in its important theory of causality. Ibn Taymiyya's enmity towards Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a blinded his insight and closed his thought. So he affirmed causes and said: 'Just as we know the association of one with the other, we know that in fire there is a power that necessitates heating, and in water a power that necessitates cooling, and likewise in the eye a power that necessitates sight, and in the tongue a power that necessitates taste.' In brief, he affirmed Aristotelian causality, affirming by his own statement—the instinct (ghariza) or the natural disposition (nakhiza), or the innate character (khuluq)—or more precisely he affirmed in the cause a 'quiddity' (mahiyya) and a 'substance' (jawhar) that is the cause of the effect. By this, Ibn Taymiyya departed from the spirit of the Islamic school and adopted the greatest ideas upon which Aristotelian logic is based. However, he returns to an interpretation of experience and the foundation upon which it is based, which brings him close to the Ash'ari theory. He holds that the word 'experience' (tajriba) is used 'for what a person experiences with his intellect and senses, even if it is not within his capabilities.' Examples: when the sun rises, light spreads; when it sets, darkness comes; when the sun is far from the zenith, cold comes; when cold comes, leaves fall, the earth's surface cools and its interior warms; when the sun is near the zenith, heat comes; when heat comes, trees leaf and blossom. Ibn Taymiyya declares that this is something all people share, for they have become accustomed to it and experienced it. But on this basis, Ibn Taymiyya holds that 'those who affirm causes' base their idea on the principle that 'a thing's similar is attracted to it, and its opposite flees from it.' He gives empirical examples to prove this. When the air cools, the earth's surface cools and what is on it cools, so heat flees to the interiors, heating the earth's core and the water within. Hence, in winter, springs are hot and animal interiors are hot, so they eat more in winter than in summer due to heat's digestion of food. In summer, the air heats, so surfaces heat and cold flees to interiors, cooling the earth's core and animal interiors, and springs cool. Thus, spring water is colder in summer than in winter, and animals' interiors are cold, so they eat less in summer due to weak digestion. These propositions are the regular empirical truths ('adadiyat mujarrabat) according to Ibn Taymiyya. He holds that experience is by one's own action and by the action of others. He says: 'A person may know from the action of others what gives him empirical knowledge, even if he does not have the power to do that action himself.' So experience is not individual; it can be the experience of others. He also declares that from the causes of experience, or 'the cause that necessitates knowledge of empirical truths, is the repetition of the association of one thing with another, either absolutely or with a sense of suitability.' Here too he applies the previous principle that experience is by one's own action and by the action of others—so the repetition of the association also...
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage critically analyzes Ibn Taymiyya's position on causality and experience. It begins by pointing out a major inconsistency: Ibn Taymiyya attacks the Ash'ari theologians for denying efficient causes and instead explaining constant conjunction as mere habit ('ada) based on God's will, yet he fails to understand that the very methods he uses (dawaran, munasaba, sabr wa taqsim) originate from the Ash'ari critique of Aristotelian causality. The author accuses Ibn Taymiyya of being blinded by his enmity towards the Ash'aris, leading him to affirm full-fledged Aristotelian causality, asserting that fire has a real power to heat, water to cool, etc. This contradicts the Islamic theological tradition that avoids attributing causal efficacy to created things. However, the author then notes that Ibn Taymiyya returns to a view closer to the Ash'ari position when explaining experience (tajriba). He defines experience as knowledge gained by repeated observation of constant conjunction, whether through one's own senses or through the testimony of others. He provides examples from natural phenomena (sun, seasons, animal digestion) to illustrate that empirical knowledge arises from repeated associations. Ibn Taymiyya even states that the cause of empirical knowledge is the repetition of conjunction, either absolutely or accompanied by a sense of appropriateness (munasaba). This brings him dangerously close to the Ash'ari theory of habit. The author highlights this as a contradiction: Ibn Taymiyya both affirms Aristotelian causal powers and grounds empirical knowledge in repeated conjunction, which is exactly the Ash'ari position he attacked.

Key Terms
الجهم
العادة
التجربة
العلية الأرسططاليسية

Ibn Taymiyya's attempt to critique Ash'ari occasionalism fails because his own account of experience ultimately relies on the same principle of repeated conjunction.

Dalil al-Ayat as Coextensiveness (Dawaran)
Author compares dalil al-ayat to the principle of coextensiveness in usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how dalil al-ayat parallels maslak al-dawaran in usul al-fiqh and how it differs from Aristotelian analogy.

Core Idea
Dalil al-ayat works like coextensiveness: a sign entails its referent, just as a cause entails its effect.

You've encountered dalil al-ayat as a distinct method of proof. This passage clarifies its nature by likening it to a well-known principle in usul al-fiqh: maslak al-dawaran (the method of coextensiveness). By understanding this parallel, you'll see why Ibn Taymiyya's method is not analogical in the Aristotelian sense but relies on necessary entailment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods being compared
Locate 'dalil al-ayat' and 'maslak al-dawaran' in the passage. Note that the author explicitly states they resemble each other.
Dawaran is a standard usul term for identifying the 'illa through coextensiveness.
2
Understand the mechanism of dawaran
Dawaran means coextensiveness in existence and non-existence: if the cause exists, the effect exists; if the cause is absent, the effect is absent. This indicates a necessary relationship.
Example: intoxication (cause) and prohibition (effect) are coextensive in Islamic law.
3
Apply this to dalil al-ayat
Similarly, a sign (aya) co-occurs with its referent in both existence and absence, establishing necessary entailment (talazum). The law of entailment (qanun al-talazum) is the underlying principle.
For instance, the sign of a miracle entails the truth of the prophet's message.
4
Contrast with Aristotelian analogy
Aristotelian analogy (tamthil) relies on shared attributes between two cases, not on necessary coextensiveness. This is a fundamentally different logical basis.
Analogy is probabilistic; dalil al-ayat claims necessity.
5
Recognize the later terminology
Later scholars called this 'law of entailment in occurrence and in absence' (qanun al-talazum fi al-wuqu' wa al-takhalluf), showing its formalization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما توضيح هذا الخلاف فاني أوضحه فيما يأتي: إن دليل الآية يشبه في الحقيقة مسلك الدوران في مباحث العلة الأصولية، أي دوران المقدم أو العلة مع التالي أو المعلول وجوداً وعدماً. وهو ما يسميه المحدثون قانون التلازم في الوقوع وفي التخلف. وهذا ما يوضح لنا الإختلاف الشديد بين طريقي الآية والتمثيل الأرسططاليسي.
As for the clarification of this difference, I will explain it as follows: The argument from signs in reality resembles the method of coextensiveness (dawaran) in the investigations of cause (al-'illa) in usul al-fiqh, that is, the coextensiveness of the antecedent or cause with the consequent or effect in existence and non-existence. That is what the later scholars call the law of entailment (talazum) in occurrence and in absence. And this shows us the profound difference between the two methods of the sign and Aristotelian analogy.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of dalil al-ayat by likening it to the principle of dawaran (coextensiveness) used in usul al-fiqh to identify the 'illa (cause). In usul, when a cause coincides with its effect in existence and absence, it indicates a necessary relationship. Similarly, dalil al-ayat relies on the entailment (talazum) between a sign and its referent. Later scholars call this the 'law of entailment in occurrence and in absence.' This comparison highlights the fundamental difference between Ibn Taymiyya's method and Aristotelian analogy, which is based on shared attributes rather than necessary entailment.

Key Terms
مسلك الدوران
قانون التلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Dalil al-ayat resembles maslak al-dawaran because both operate on the principle of necessary coextensiveness in existence and non-existence, distinguishing them from Aristotelian analogy.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Universal Propositions
Critique of universal propositions in metaphysics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's objection to universal propositions in metaphysical demonstration and identify its sources of influence.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya denied real universals, challenging the foundation of Aristotelian demonstration in metaphysics.

In his critique of Aristotelian logic, Ibn Taymiyya targeted the universal proposition (al-qaḍiyya al-kulliyya), especially when applied to metaphysical matters. Influenced by skeptics and Stoics, he argued that universals are not real and that demonstration should not rely on them. This lesson unpacks his critique and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target
Locate the passage where the author discusses Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the universal proposition. Note that it is in the context of applying demonstration to 'matters of existence' (mabāḥith al-wujūd).
2
Find the influences
The passage mentions influence from skeptics and Stoics in this critique. Identify these influences as external sources for Ibn Taymiyya's denial of universals.
3
Understand the metaphysical context
Ibn Taymiyya entered into investigations of existence, its divisions, essences, and their existence. He denied universals and retained only sensible particulars. Reflect on why this matters for metaphysics.
4
Assess the scope of his critique
The author notes that Ibn Taymiyya did NOT criticize the confinement of demonstration to two premises or the limitation of syllogistic forms to three. This shows his critique was selective.
This is important for evaluating the completeness of his critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بهذا ينتهي الجانب الموجب من نقد ابن تيمية للقياس الأرسططاليسي ونستطيع الآن أن نلمس في هذا النقد طرافة وابتكاراً . فهو لم يقف عند حد نقد الشكاك . بل وضع هو أيضاً نقوداً عدة ، صدر فيها عن فكر خاص ، بحيث يبدو أثر الشكاك في هذا الفصل ضئيلاً باهتاً . حقاً إنه تأثر بهم من ناحية وبالرواقية من ناحية في نقده الطويل للقضية الكلية ، وخاصة حين حاول أن يطبق البرهان على مباحث الوجود . ودخل بهذا في أبحاث ميتافيزيقية كالوجود وأقسامه والماهيات ووجودها ، فأنكر الكليات ولم يستبق إلى الجزئيات المحسوسة . ولكن لا نجد شيئاً من نقده لاقتصار البرهان على مقدمتين أو لحصر صور الإستدلال في ثلاثة في التراث الهليني . أما ارتباط عناصر هذا النقد التفصيلي بنقده العام للمنطق فارتباط وثيق . وفي إيجاز يقدم لنا ابن تيمية في هذا الفصل مبحثاً كاملاً متفق العناصر ، متناسق الأجزاء . محاولاً أن يثبت به أن المنطق ليس مخالفاً فحسب لصحيح المنقول بل لصريح المعقول . أما عن الطرق العقلية التي وضعها لكي يستخدمها النظار ، فقد رأينا أن كلاً منهما يقوم على فكرة من أعمق الأفكار المنطقية . فالطريق الأول يعود كما قلنا إلى قانون التلازم والتخلف في الوقوع . والطريق الثاني يعود إلى فهم وتفريق دقيق بين الأسماء المشككة والمتواطئة أو بين المتواطئة العامة والمتواطئة الخاصة . وبهذا حقق ابن تيمية فكرته القائلة بأن للمسلمين طرقاً خاصة في البحث ، مستمدة من القرآن وقائمة عليه . وأخيراً ترك ابن تيمية بما كتبه عن المنطق تراثاً علمياً لا يقدر . وقام بمحاولة لا تجد لها شبيهاً في تاريخ العصور الوسطى ، بحيث يقول الأستاذ مصطفى عبد الرازق « إن الدراسات المنطقية لو سارت منذ عهد ابن تيمية على نهجه في النقد بدل الشرح والتعمق ، لكنا بلغنا بها من الرقي مبلغاً عظيماً » . هذا هو التراث المنطقي التيمي ، فما هو أثره فيمن جاء بعده من مفكرين ؟ هذا ما سنبحثه في الفصل القادم .
Thus ends the positive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian analogical reasoning, and we can now perceive in this critique originality and innovation. He did not stop at the criticism of the skeptics. Rather, he also made several criticisms, issuing from his own thought, such that the influence of the skeptics in this chapter appears faint and weak. Indeed, he was influenced by them on one hand and by the Stoics on the other in his lengthy critique of the universal proposition, especially when he tried to apply demonstration to matters of existence. And by this he entered into metaphysical investigations such as existence and its divisions, essences and their existence, so he denied universals and did not retain particulars except for the sensible. But we do not find anything of his criticism of confining demonstration to two premises or confining the forms of inference to three in the Hellenic heritage. As for the connection of the elements of this detailed critique with his general critique of logic, it is a strong connection. And in summary, Ibn Taymiyya presents to us in this chapter a complete investigation, congruent in its elements, harmonious in its parts, attempting to prove that logic is not only contrary to sound transmitted knowledge but also to clear rational knowledge. As for the rational methods he established for the theorists to use, we have seen that each of them is based on one of the deepest logical ideas. The first method goes back, as we said, to the law of correlation and implication in occurrence. The second method goes back to a precise understanding and distinction between equivocal and univocal names or between general univocal and specific univocal. And with this, Ibn Taymiyya realized his idea that Muslims have special methods of investigation, derived from the Quran and based upon it. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya left, through what he wrote about logic, an invaluable scholarly legacy. And he undertook an attempt unparalleled in the history of the Middle Ages, such that Professor Mustafa Abd al-Raziq said: 'If logical studies had proceeded since the time of Ibn Taymiyya on his path of criticism instead of commentary and depth, we would have reached a great level of progress.' This is the logical legacy of Ibn Taymiyya, so what is its effect on those who came after him among thinkers? This is what we will research in the next chapter.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a concluding evaluation of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic by a modern scholar. It opens by stating that the positive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique ends, highlighting its originality beyond the influence of earlier skeptics. The author notes that Ibn Taymiyya was influenced by the skeptics and Stoics in his critique of universal propositions when applying demonstration to metaphysical topics like existence and essences, leading him to deny universals except as sensible particulars. However, the author points out that Ibn Taymiyya did not criticize the confinement of demonstration to two premises or the limitation of syllogistic forms to three—elements from Hellenic tradition. The connection between his detailed critique and his general critique of logic is strong. Ibn Taymiyya's chapter presents a coherent investigation aimed at proving that logic contradicts both transmitted and rational knowledge. The passage then describes Ibn Taymiyya's two rational methods for theorists: the first relies on the law of correlation and implication regarding occurrence (necessary concomitance), and the second involves a precise distinction between equivocal (ambiguous) and univocal terms, or between general and specific univocals. This actualizes Ibn Taymiyya's idea that Muslims have their own research methods derived from the Quran. Finally, the author emphasizes Ibn Taymiyya's invaluable legacy, quoting Mustafa Abd al-Raziq that logical studies would have advanced greatly if they followed Ibn Taymiyya's critical path instead of mere commentary. The passage ends by hinting at the next chapter on the impact of Ibn Taymiyya's legacy on later thinkers.

Ibn Taymiyya's Two Rational Methods
Presentation of Ibn Taymiyya's alternative rational methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe Ibn Taymiyya's two rational methods (talāzum/takhalluf and distinction of names) and apply them to a simple theological example.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya replaced Aristotelian syllogism with two Quranic rational methods: correlation and precise naming.

Despite his fierce critique of Aristotelian logic, Ibn Taymiyya did not abandon rational inquiry. He proposed two original methods for theorists, both derived from the Quran and designed to avoid the pitfalls of Greek logic. This lesson explores these methods.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first method: talāzum wa takhalluf
This is the law of correlation and implication in occurrence. It examines whether two things always coexist (talāzum) or whether the existence of one implies the other (takhalluf). This is used to establish necessary connections between concepts.
It is akin to a conditional reasoning but grounded in real relationships.
2
Understand the second method: distinguishing mushakkik and mutawāṭi'
This method requires a precise analysis of terms: equivocal (mushakkik) names apply to multiple things with different degrees (e.g., 'light' for sun and lamp), while univocal (mutawāṭi') names apply equally. Within univocals, there are general and specific types. This helps avoid confusion in reasoning.
Ibn Taymiyya likely used this to clarify terms like 'wujūd' (existence) which is applied to God and creatures equivocally.
3
Recognize the Quranic foundation
The passage says these methods are 'derived from the Quran and based upon it'. This means Ibn Taymiyya saw these as Islamic alternatives to Greek logic.
He believed Muslims have their own unique rational tools.
4
Apply to a theological example
Consider the relationship between 'the world is created' and 'the world is temporally originated'. Using talāzum, one might argue they are correlated. Using the name distinction, 'creator' applied to God vs. humans is equivocal.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a concluding evaluation of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic by a modern scholar. It opens by stating that the positive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique ends, highlighting its originality beyond the influence of earlier skeptics. The author notes that Ibn Taymiyya was influenced by the skeptics and Stoics in his critique of universal propositions when applying demonstration to metaphysical topics like existence and essences, leading him to deny universals except as sensible particulars. However, the author points out that Ibn Taymiyya did not criticize the confinement of demonstration to two premises or the limitation of syllogistic forms to three—elements from Hellenic tradition. The connection between his detailed critique and his general critique of logic is strong. Ibn Taymiyya's chapter presents a coherent investigation aimed at proving that logic contradicts both transmitted and rational knowledge. The passage then describes Ibn Taymiyya's two rational methods for theorists: the first relies on the law of correlation and implication regarding occurrence (necessary concomitance), and the second involves a precise distinction between equivocal (ambiguous) and univocal terms, or between general and specific univocals. This actualizes Ibn Taymiyya's idea that Muslims have their own research methods derived from the Quran. Finally, the author emphasizes Ibn Taymiyya's invaluable legacy, quoting Mustafa Abd al-Raziq that logical studies would have advanced greatly if they followed Ibn Taymiyya's critical path instead of mere commentary. The passage ends by hinting at the next chapter on the impact of Ibn Taymiyya's legacy on later thinkers.

Evaluating Ibn Taymiyya's Logical Legacy
Final evaluation of Ibn Taymiyya's logical critique and methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's contribution to logic as presented in the passage and discuss its potential impact.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya's original critique could have revolutionized Islamic logic if it had been followed.

This concluding passage evaluates Ibn Taymiyya's overall contribution. It praises his originality but also notes its selective nature. The lesson will help you assess his legacy and the road not taken in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify what the author praises
The author uses words like 'originality' (ṭarāfa) and 'innovation' (ibtikār). Note that Ibn Taymiyya went beyond the skeptics and created his own critique.
2
Note what he did not criticize
The author points out that Ibn Taymiyya failed to criticize the confinement of demonstration to two premises and the limitation of syllogistic forms to three. This shows his critique was incomplete.
3
Understand the quoted praise
Mustafa Abd al-Raziq is quoted saying that if logical studies had followed Ibn Taymiyya's critical path instead of commentary, they would have advanced greatly. This is a hypothetical counterfactual.
4
Reflect on the legacy question
The passage ends with a question: 'What is the effect of his legacy on later thinkers?' This invites us to consider the historical impact.

بهذا ينتهي الجانب الموجب من نقد ابن تيمية للقياس الأرسططاليسي ونستطيع الآن أن نلمس في هذا النقد طرافة وابتكاراً . فهو لم يقف عند حد نقد الشكاك . بل وضع هو أيضاً نقوداً عدة ، صدر فيها عن فكر خاص ، بحيث يبدو أثر الشكاك في هذا الفصل ضئيلاً باهتاً . حقاً إنه تأثر بهم من ناحية وبالرواقية من ناحية في نقده الطويل للقضية الكلية ، وخاصة حين حاول أن يطبق البرهان على مباحث الوجود . ودخل بهذا في أبحاث ميتافيزيقية كالوجود وأقسامه والماهيات ووجودها ، فأنكر الكليات ولم يستبق إلى الجزئيات المحسوسة . ولكن لا نجد شيئاً من نقده لاقتصار البرهان على مقدمتين أو لحصر صور الإستدلال في ثلاثة في التراث الهليني . أما ارتباط عناصر هذا النقد التفصيلي بنقده العام للمنطق فارتباط وثيق . وفي إيجاز يقدم لنا ابن تيمية في هذا الفصل مبحثاً كاملاً متفق العناصر ، متناسق الأجزاء . محاولاً أن يثبت به أن المنطق ليس مخالفاً فحسب لصحيح المنقول بل لصريح المعقول . أما عن الطرق العقلية التي وضعها لكي يستخدمها النظار ، فقد رأينا أن كلاً منهما يقوم على فكرة من أعمق الأفكار المنطقية . فالطريق الأول يعود كما قلنا إلى قانون التلازم والتخلف في الوقوع . والطريق الثاني يعود إلى فهم وتفريق دقيق بين الأسماء المشككة والمتواطئة أو بين المتواطئة العامة والمتواطئة الخاصة . وبهذا حقق ابن تيمية فكرته القائلة بأن للمسلمين طرقاً خاصة في البحث ، مستمدة من القرآن وقائمة عليه . وأخيراً ترك ابن تيمية بما كتبه عن المنطق تراثاً علمياً لا يقدر . وقام بمحاولة لا تجد لها شبيهاً في تاريخ العصور الوسطى ، بحيث يقول الأستاذ مصطفى عبد الرازق « إن الدراسات المنطقية لو سارت منذ عهد ابن تيمية على نهجه في النقد بدل الشرح والتعمق ، لكنا بلغنا بها من الرقي مبلغاً عظيماً » . هذا هو التراث المنطقي التيمي ، فما هو أثره فيمن جاء بعده من مفكرين ؟ هذا ما سنبحثه في الفصل القادم .
Thus ends the positive aspect of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian analogical reasoning, and we can now perceive in this critique originality and innovation. He did not stop at the criticism of the skeptics. Rather, he also made several criticisms, issuing from his own thought, such that the influence of the skeptics in this chapter appears faint and weak. Indeed, he was influenced by them on one hand and by the Stoics on the other in his lengthy critique of the universal proposition, especially when he tried to apply demonstration to matters of existence. And by this he entered into metaphysical investigations such as existence and its divisions, essences and their existence, so he denied universals and did not retain particulars except for the sensible. But we do not find anything of his criticism of confining demonstration to two premises or confining the forms of inference to three in the Hellenic heritage. As for the connection of the elements of this detailed critique with his general critique of logic, it is a strong connection. And in summary, Ibn Taymiyya presents to us in this chapter a complete investigation, congruent in its elements, harmonious in its parts, attempting to prove that logic is not only contrary to sound transmitted knowledge but also to clear rational knowledge. As for the rational methods he established for the theorists to use, we have seen that each of them is based on one of the deepest logical ideas. The first method goes back, as we said, to the law of correlation and implication in occurrence. The second method goes back to a precise understanding and distinction between equivocal and univocal names or between general univocal and specific univocal. And with this, Ibn Taymiyya realized his idea that Muslims have special methods of investigation, derived from the Quran and based upon it. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya left, through what he wrote about logic, an invaluable scholarly legacy. And he undertook an attempt unparalleled in the history of the Middle Ages, such that Professor Mustafa Abd al-Raziq said: 'If logical studies had proceeded since the time of Ibn Taymiyya on his path of criticism instead of commentary and depth, we would have reached a great level of progress.' This is the logical legacy of Ibn Taymiyya, so what is its effect on those who came after him among thinkers? This is what we will research in the next chapter.

Suhrawardi's Dual Stance on Aristotelian Logic
Author describes Suhrawardi's acceptance and reform of Aristotelian logic via illumination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Suhrawardi's dual stance toward Aristotelian logic and explain how he distinguishes himself from both Peripatetics and other Sufis.

Core Idea
Suhrawardi accepts logic as spiritual exercise, then reforms it into a condensed illuminationist system.

Suhrawardi occupies a unique position in Islamic intellectual history: he does not entirely reject Aristotelian logic like many Sufis, nor does he accept it uncritically like the Peripatetics. Instead, he adopts it as a spiritual discipline and then transforms it into a concise, illuminationist logic derived from taste and unveiling. This lesson unpacks his dual stance and the motivations behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides of Suhrawardi's stance
Find where the passage shows that Suhrawardi both accepts logic in general and then proposes a new condensed version. This is his dual stance.
2
Contrast with other Sufis
The passage states that Suhrawardi differed from Sufis who did not accept any form of theoretical thought. Notice how he considers logic a spiritual exercise.
3
Note his criticism of Ibn Sina and Aristotle
Identify the sentences where Suhrawardi attacks Ibn Sina for his Peripateticism and claims that adhering to Aristotle does not lead to true knowledge.
4
Understand the role of taste (dhawq)
Suhrawardi declares that he invented his logic through taste. Locate this claim and understand that taste is an intuitive, non-discursive source.
5
Recognize the goal of condensed logic
The passage says Suhrawardi aims to make logic 'condensed, controlled by few controls with abundant benefits.' This is the outcome of his reform.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصلوا إلى المعارف الحقة ، ما داموا حرموا من طريق الكشف والمعاينة وإشراق المعارف لم يعرفوا الحكمة المؤسسة على الإشراق ، الكشف ، حكمة المشارقة من أهل فارس . إن حكمتهم كشفية ذوقية ، فنسبت إلى الإشراق الذي هو ظهور الأنوار العقلية ولمعانها وفيضانها بالإشراقات على الأنفس عند تجردها . « وكان اعتماد الفارسيين في الحكمة على الذوق والكشف ، وكذا قدماء اليونان ، خلا أرسطو وشيعته فان اعتمادهم كان على البحث والبرهان لا غير »(۱) ، ويهاجم السهروردي ابن سينا لمشائيته ولمهاجمته لأفلاطون ويرى أن الالتحام بأرسطو لا يؤدي إلى المعرفة الحقة . غير أن الإشراقية اتخذت بالرغم من هذا مبدأ لها دراسة المنطق الأرسططاليسي ، بل جعلت دراسته رياضة روحية ، وقد أدى هذا إلى عملية إصلاح كبيرة في المنطق الأرسططاليسي (٢) . الفَصْلُ الثَّانِي المنطق الإشراقي عند السهروردي قلنا إن للسهروردي تجاه المنطق الأرسططاليسي موقفاً مزدوجاً ـ فهو يقبل المنطق عامة ، ويعتبره إحدى رياضات المتصوفة الإشراقية ثم يضع منطقاً جديداً ثانياً ، أو يحاول أن يختصر هذا المنطق ثانياً . وقلنا إنه بهذا يخالف الصوفية الذين لم يقبلوا أي صورة من صور التفكير النظري . وإن كان السهروردي نفسه يصرح بأنه توصل إلى ابتكار هذا المنطق بواسطة الذوق ، ويسمي كثيراً من أبحاثه الجديدة ضوابط إشراقية . فيقرر أنه في حكمة الإشراق يبدأ على سياق يبتني على الذوق والكشف ومشاهدة الأنوار بخلاف سياق المشائين الذي يبتني على البحث الصرف . ويصف هذا السياق الصوفي بأنه « سياق آخر وطريق أقرب من تلك الطريقة وأنظم وأضبط وأقل أتعاباً في التحصيل »(۱) ويقرر في فقرة أخرى أنه سيجعل الآلة الواقية للفكر ، أي المنطق مختصرة مضبوطة بضوابط قليلة العدد كثيرة الفوائد(۲) . ويشرح الشارح هذا المنطق الإشراقي بأنه موجز حذفت منه الفروع الكثيرة القليلة الاستعمال ، وأن السهروردي بين فيه أشياء كانت في طريقتهم غير محصلة ولا مهذبة ، ويصف السهروردي كيفية حصوله على هذا المنطق الموجز فيقول « لم يحصل لي أولاً بالفكر بل كان حصوله بأمر آخر ـ ثم طلبت الحجة
They do not arrive at true cognitions, as long as they are deprived of the path of unveiling and direct vision, and the illumination of cognitions. They did not know wisdom founded on illumination, unveiling, the wisdom of the Oriental sages from Persia. Their wisdom is unveiling and taste, so it is attributed to illumination, which is the appearance of intellectual lights, their radiance, and their outpouring by illuminations upon souls when they become abstract. 'The Persians relied in wisdom on taste and unveiling, as did the ancient Greeks, except Aristotle and his followers, for their reliance was on research and demonstration alone.' Suhrawardi attacks Ibn Sina for his Peripateticism and for his attack on Plato, and he sees that adhering to Aristotle does not lead to true knowledge. However, the Illuminationists, despite this, adopted as their principle the study of Aristotelian logic, and even made its study a spiritual exercise, and this led to a great reform in Aristotelian logic. Chapter Two: Illuminationist Logic according to Suhrawardi. We have said that Suhrawardi has a dual stance toward Aristotelian logic: he accepts logic in general and considers it one of the exercises of the Illuminationist Sufis, then he proposes a new logic in the second place, or attempts to condense this logic in the second place. And we said that by this he differed from the Sufis who did not accept any form of theoretical thought. Although Suhrawardi himself declares that he arrived at the invention of this logic through taste, and he calls many of his new researches illuminationist controls. He states that in the Philosophy of Illumination he begins on a method based on taste, unveiling, and the observation of lights, contrary to the method of the Peripatetics which is based on pure research. He describes this Sufi method as 'another method and a nearer path than that method, more orderly, more precise, and less tiresome in acquisition.' And in another passage he declares that he will make the protective instrument of thought, that is logic, condensed, controlled by few controls with abundant benefits. And the commentator explains this Illuminationist logic as an abridgment from which many seldom-used branches have been omitted, and in which Suhrawardi clarified things that in their method were not refined or improved. And Suhrawardi describes how he obtained this condensed logic, saying: 'It did not first come to me by thought, but rather its acquisition was by another matter—then I sought the proof.'
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a critical and historical exposition of Suhrawardi's stance toward Aristotelian logic and his development of Illuminationist logic. The author begins by contrasting the Persian Illuminationists (who rely on taste and unveiling) with Aristotle and his followers (who rely solely on research and demonstration). Suhrawardi criticizes Ibn Sina for his Peripateticism and for attacking Plato, asserting that clinging to Aristotle does not yield true knowledge. Despite this critique, the Illuminationists adopted Aristotelian logic as a spiritual exercise, leading to a reform. The author then introduces Suhrawardi's dual position: he accepts logic in general as a spiritual exercise for Illuminationist Sufis, and then develops a new condensed logic. This distinguishes him from other Sufis who reject all theoretical thought. Suhrawardi claims his logic came through taste (dhawq), not mere thought, and he calls its principles 'illuminationist controls' (dawabit ishraqiyya). In his 'Philosophy of Illumination,' he follows a method based on taste, unveiling, and observation of lights, which he describes as more orderly and precise than the Peripatetic method. He aims to make logic concise with few but highly beneficial principles. The commentator explains that this condensed logic omits many seldom-used branches and clarifies previously unrefined elements. Suhrawardi's own account: his logic was not first obtained by thought but through another means, after which he sought proof. This passage highlights Suhrawardi's unique synthesis of mystical experience and logical discipline.

Key Terms
المنطق الإشراقي (al-mantiq al-ishraqi): The condensed, reformed logic based on illumination and taste, contrasted with Aristotelian logic. [Note: This term is specific to Suhrawardi's school; later philosophers may not use it.]
الذوق (al-dhawq): Taste; a direct, intuitive mode of knowledge that Suhrawardi claims as the source of his logical innovations. [Note: In Sufi contexts, dhawq often means spiritual taste; Suhrawardi applies it to logical discovery.]
الكشف (al-kashf): Unveiling; a method of direct spiritual vision opposed to theoretical research. [Note: Standard term in Islamic mysticism; here used to justify a logical system.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Suhrawardi's dual stance—accepting then reforming Aristotelian logic—sets him apart from both Peripatetics and other Sufis.

The Illuminationist Alternative: Taste and Unveiling as Sources of Logic
Author describes Suhrawardi's acceptance and reform of Aristotelian logic via illumination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how Suhrawardi's illuminationist logic differs from Aristotelian logic in its epistemological foundations (dhawq, kashf).

Core Idea
Suhrawardi's logic is founded on taste and unveiling, not on pure research.

Suhrawardi contrasts his method with the Peripatetics: while they rely on research and demonstration, he bases his logic on taste (dhawq) and unveiling (kashf). He claims his condensed logic came to him by a means other than thought, and only afterward did he seek proof. This lesson explores the epistemological shift he proposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods contrasted
Find where the passage contrasts the Peripatetic method (based on pure research) with the Illuminationist method (based on taste and unveiling).
2
Locate Suhrawardi's claim about acquiring logic through taste
Suhrawardi declares that he invented his logic through taste and calls its principles 'illuminationist controls.'
3
Note his description of the illuminationist method
He describes his method as 'another method and a nearer path... more orderly, more precise, and less tiresome.'
4
Understand the role of 'illuminationist controls'
These are the condensed principles of his logic, derived from dhawq. They are few but highly beneficial.
5
Reflect on the epistemological priority
Suhrawardi claims that his logic came first through taste, then he sought proof. This reverses the usual priority of discursive reason over intuition.

يصلوا إلى المعارف الحقة ، ما داموا حرموا من طريق الكشف والمعاينة وإشراق المعارف لم يعرفوا الحكمة المؤسسة على الإشراق ، الكشف ، حكمة المشارقة من أهل فارس . إن حكمتهم كشفية ذوقية ، فنسبت إلى الإشراق الذي هو ظهور الأنوار العقلية ولمعانها وفيضانها بالإشراقات على الأنفس عند تجردها . « وكان اعتماد الفارسيين في الحكمة على الذوق والكشف ، وكذا قدماء اليونان ، خلا أرسطو وشيعته فان اعتمادهم كان على البحث والبرهان لا غير »(۱) ، ويهاجم السهروردي ابن سينا لمشائيته ولمهاجمته لأفلاطون ويرى أن الالتحام بأرسطو لا يؤدي إلى المعرفة الحقة . غير أن الإشراقية اتخذت بالرغم من هذا مبدأ لها دراسة المنطق الأرسططاليسي ، بل جعلت دراسته رياضة روحية ، وقد أدى هذا إلى عملية إصلاح كبيرة في المنطق الأرسططاليسي (٢) . الفَصْلُ الثَّانِي المنطق الإشراقي عند السهروردي قلنا إن للسهروردي تجاه المنطق الأرسططاليسي موقفاً مزدوجاً ـ فهو يقبل المنطق عامة ، ويعتبره إحدى رياضات المتصوفة الإشراقية ثم يضع منطقاً جديداً ثانياً ، أو يحاول أن يختصر هذا المنطق ثانياً . وقلنا إنه بهذا يخالف الصوفية الذين لم يقبلوا أي صورة من صور التفكير النظري . وإن كان السهروردي نفسه يصرح بأنه توصل إلى ابتكار هذا المنطق بواسطة الذوق ، ويسمي كثيراً من أبحاثه الجديدة ضوابط إشراقية . فيقرر أنه في حكمة الإشراق يبدأ على سياق يبتني على الذوق والكشف ومشاهدة الأنوار بخلاف سياق المشائين الذي يبتني على البحث الصرف . ويصف هذا السياق الصوفي بأنه « سياق آخر وطريق أقرب من تلك الطريقة وأنظم وأضبط وأقل أتعاباً في التحصيل »(۱) ويقرر في فقرة أخرى أنه سيجعل الآلة الواقية للفكر ، أي المنطق مختصرة مضبوطة بضوابط قليلة العدد كثيرة الفوائد(۲) . ويشرح الشارح هذا المنطق الإشراقي بأنه موجز حذفت منه الفروع الكثيرة القليلة الاستعمال ، وأن السهروردي بين فيه أشياء كانت في طريقتهم غير محصلة ولا مهذبة ، ويصف السهروردي كيفية حصوله على هذا المنطق الموجز فيقول « لم يحصل لي أولاً بالفكر بل كان حصوله بأمر آخر ـ ثم طلبت الحجة
They do not arrive at true cognitions, as long as they are deprived of the path of unveiling and direct vision, and the illumination of cognitions. They did not know wisdom founded on illumination, unveiling, the wisdom of the Oriental sages from Persia. Their wisdom is unveiling and taste, so it is attributed to illumination, which is the appearance of intellectual lights, their radiance, and their outpouring by illuminations upon souls when they become abstract. 'The Persians relied in wisdom on taste and unveiling, as did the ancient Greeks, except Aristotle and his followers, for their reliance was on research and demonstration alone.' Suhrawardi attacks Ibn Sina for his Peripateticism and for his attack on Plato, and he sees that adhering to Aristotle does not lead to true knowledge. However, the Illuminationists, despite this, adopted as their principle the study of Aristotelian logic, and even made its study a spiritual exercise, and this led to a great reform in Aristotelian logic. Chapter Two: Illuminationist Logic according to Suhrawardi. We have said that Suhrawardi has a dual stance toward Aristotelian logic: he accepts logic in general and considers it one of the exercises of the Illuminationist Sufis, then he proposes a new logic in the second place, or attempts to condense this logic in the second place. And we said that by this he differed from the Sufis who did not accept any form of theoretical thought. Although Suhrawardi himself declares that he arrived at the invention of this logic through taste, and he calls many of his new researches illuminationist controls. He states that in the Philosophy of Illumination he begins on a method based on taste, unveiling, and the observation of lights, contrary to the method of the Peripatetics which is based on pure research. He describes this Sufi method as 'another method and a nearer path than that method, more orderly, more precise, and less tiresome in acquisition.' And in another passage he declares that he will make the protective instrument of thought, that is logic, condensed, controlled by few controls with abundant benefits. And the commentator explains this Illuminationist logic as an abridgment from which many seldom-used branches have been omitted, and in which Suhrawardi clarified things that in their method were not refined or improved. And Suhrawardi describes how he obtained this condensed logic, saying: 'It did not first come to me by thought, but rather its acquisition was by another matter—then I sought the proof.'
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a critical and historical exposition of Suhrawardi's stance toward Aristotelian logic and his development of Illuminationist logic. The author begins by contrasting the Persian Illuminationists (who rely on taste and unveiling) with Aristotle and his followers (who rely solely on research and demonstration). Suhrawardi criticizes Ibn Sina for his Peripateticism and for attacking Plato, asserting that clinging to Aristotle does not yield true knowledge. Despite this critique, the Illuminationists adopted Aristotelian logic as a spiritual exercise, leading to a reform. The author then introduces Suhrawardi's dual position: he accepts logic in general as a spiritual exercise for Illuminationist Sufis, and then develops a new condensed logic. This distinguishes him from other Sufis who reject all theoretical thought. Suhrawardi claims his logic came through taste (dhawq), not mere thought, and he calls its principles 'illuminationist controls' (dawabit ishraqiyya). In his 'Philosophy of Illumination,' he follows a method based on taste, unveiling, and observation of lights, which he describes as more orderly and precise than the Peripatetic method. He aims to make logic concise with few but highly beneficial principles. The commentator explains that this condensed logic omits many seldom-used branches and clarifies previously unrefined elements. Suhrawardi's own account: his logic was not first obtained by thought but through another means, after which he sought proof. This passage highlights Suhrawardi's unique synthesis of mystical experience and logical discipline.

Key Terms
المنطق الإشراقي (al-mantiq al-ishraqi): The condensed, reformed logic based on illumination and taste, contrasted with Aristotelian logic. [Note: This term is specific to Suhrawardi's school; later philosophers may not use it.]
الذوق (al-dhawq): Taste; a direct, intuitive mode of knowledge that Suhrawardi claims as the source of his logical innovations. [Note: In Sufi contexts, dhawq often means spiritual taste; Suhrawardi applies it to logical discovery.]
الكشف (al-kashf): Unveiling; a method of direct spiritual vision opposed to theoretical research. [Note: Standard term in Islamic mysticism; here used to justify a logical system.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how did Suhrawardi acquire his condensed logic?
A) Through pure thought and demonstration.
B) Through taste and unveiling (dhawq), then he sought the proof.
C) Through studying Aristotle's works.
D) Through debate with other philosophers.

Key Takeaway
Suhrawardi's epistemology of logic prioritizes intuitive taste over discursive thought, but does not abandon proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Compare Suhrawardi's method of acquiring logic (via taste, then proof) with the Aristotelian method of demonstration. Write two sentences explaining the difference.

Suhrawardi's Critique of Peripatetic Logic and His Reform
Author describes Suhrawardi's acceptance and reform of Aristotelian logic via illumination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Suhrawardi's critique of Aristotelian logic and the reforms he proposes.

Core Idea
Suhrawardi criticizes the Peripatetics for overcomplicating logic and proposes a condensed, more beneficial system.

While Suhrawardi accepts logic, he is critical of the Peripatetic tradition, especially Ibn Sina. He argues that their method of pure research does not lead to true knowledge and that their logic is needlessly elaborate. His reform aims to condense logic into few but highly beneficial principles, derived from illuminationist insights. This lesson examines his critique and the nature of his reforms.

Guided Path
1
Locate Suhrawardi's attack on Ibn Sina and Aristotle
Find the sentence where Suhrawardi attacks Ibn Sina for his Peripateticism and states that adhering to Aristotle does not lead to true knowledge.
2
Identify his claim that pure research is insufficient
The passage contrasts the Peripatetic method (pure research) with the illuminationist method (taste/unveiling). Suhrawardi claims the former does not yield true knowledge.
3
Note the description of his reform
Suhrawardi declares he will make logic 'condensed, controlled by few controls with abundant benefits.'
4
Understand the commentator's explanation of the reform
The commentator says the Illuminationist logic is an abridgment that omits seldom-used branches and clarifies unrefined elements.
5
Evaluate the dual reaction: acceptance then reform
Suhrawardi does not reject logic outright; he accepts it as a spiritual exercise and then reforms it. This is a critical but constructive stance.
